Preface 
This project was written as a master thesis at Technical University of Denmark, Architectural 
Engineering, in collaboration White Architects AB. The Project is credited 30 ECTS and performed 
during September 2015 to March 2016. Lotte Bjerregaard Jensen and Toke Rammer Nielsen have 
been the main supervisors from DTU BYG. Elise Grosse from the Environmental department at 
White Architects, Stockholm has worked as external supervisor and contact person to interested 
parts.  
The project was handed in at the 7th of March 2016 
 
 
______________________ 
 
Mikkel Kirkeskov Knudsen 
s093360 
 
 
 
==PAGE:4==
1.1 - Introduction 
Page 2 
 
Abstract 
The existing building stock constitutes a major part of the consumed energy within Nordic 
countries. Most residential buildings from the 1960's and 1970's including the Swedish 
Miljonprogramme housing, stands out with poor energy performance and most often with deprived 
urban environment. An alternative solution to conventional refurbishment of these buildings is 
needed, that can improve the energy performance and at the same time make basis for a whole new 
sustainable environment with respect to both the local environment as well as the global challenges 
we are facing regarding global warming. 
The Eco-Canopy proposal by White Architects, proposed for the Swedish competition “Innovative 
utilisation of excess heat for future cities” in June 2015, included an idea of adding an insulating 
transparent canopy between two multi-storey residential buildings and thereby creating a semi-
conditioned semi-outdoor space that seeks to embrace essential challenges associated with the large 
amount of existing residential apartment buildings in Miljonprogrammet. The concept aims to 
improve energy performance of existing buildings by lowering heat losses from the facades facing 
the canopy and by that avoid the extensive refurbishment of the concerning facades. 
The goal of the thesis is to investigate the EKO-Canopy concept from a technical perspective using 
an integrated approach to deal with the multiple technical challenges of the concept - if possible, 
bring the concept closer to realization by making a basis for further development of the EKO-
Canopy. Dragonvägen Swedish Miljonprogram apartment buildings are used as case study. 
Investigations include research about alternative ways of designing the EKO-Canopy, including a 
research about using ETFE cushions instead of the proposed glass construction. Furthermore the 
investigations include geometry findings by comparison of suggested solutions, regarding surface 
area for thermal transmission and material usage and forms that can deal with critical snow and wind 
loads. Wind flow simulations are performed as a part of these investigations. 
Daylight investigations are a major part of the investigations to clarify to what extend an EKO-
Canopy reduces incoming daylight to the existing apartments that faces the canopy. Simulations of 
daylight are performed with Velux Daylight Visualizer with regards to Daylight Factor assessed with 
Nordic building regulations, with the Swedish standard SS 914201 being the main focus for the 
investigations. Parameter investigations show possible solutions to be able to deal with the 
challenging daylight conditions.  
Energy performance and indoor climate investigations are performed with IES Virtual Environment 
dynamic building simulation tool. Various scenarios are investigated on basis of annual energy 
consumption and thermal indoor climate of the canopy space. Scenarios include a principle of 
leading exhaust air from existing apartments through a heat exchanger coupled to the canopy supply 
air and using the canopy as a supply air duct for the apartments. Further scenarios include additional 
renovation of the buildings in combination with the EKO-Canopy concept. Results show that the 
best energy performance is achieved if the entire apartment supply air can be supplied from the 
canopy space during times with heating needs.  
Simulations show that a favourable thermal indoor climate can be achieved within the canopy space 
by only using passive strategies of heating and cooling. However, high temperatures due to radiant 
heat result in high temperatures within the occupied zone of the EKO-Canopy, as no shading was 
included in the simulations. 
The studies show good potential for the EKO-Canopy concept to be a profitable alternative to 
conventional renovation, when considering the possibilities for the new passively conditioned space 
between the existing buildings.

1.1 - Introduction

 INTRODUCTION 
PART 1:
1 
Introduction 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Many projects and investigations of 3rd room spaces, including atriums and canopies has been done 
before. This report seeks to investigate the wide perspectives of the EKO-Canopy concept in order 
to speed up the process of a potential realisation. By approaching the project with a holistic 
integrated design method it is the goal to include as many important technical aspects as possible, 
that can guide the project in its initial design phase.  
The idea is that the thesis can be used as a catalogue for the further development of the EKO-
Canopy concept. Therefore the thesis include broad perspectives of the concept as it is still in its 
early design phase, where many challenges are to be solved.  
The thesis is based on a case study of Dragonvägen multi-storey apartment buildings from the 
Swedish Million Homes Program (Miljonprogrammet) 
  
 
 
 
 
“Sustainable architecture is a general term that describes 
environmentally conscious design techniques in the field of architecture. 
Sustainable architecture is framed by the larger discussion on 
sustainability and the pressing economic and political issues of our 
world. In the broad context, it seeks to minimize the negative 
environmental impact of buildings by enhancing efficiency and 
moderation in the use of materials, energy, and development space.“ 
(Nichols & Mackenzie 2011) 
==PAGE:11==
1.1 - Introduction 
Page 9 
 
1.1 
EKO-Canopy by White Architects  
The EKO-Canopy proposal by White Architects include installation of a canopy covering between 
existing Miljonprogram (English: Swedish Million Homes Program) apartment building blocks. The 
goal is to create a sheltered and conditioned space for social activities combined with energy savings 
due to reduced heat losses of the adjacent building blocks. Excess heat from industrial processes is 
proposed used in combination with STES (Seasonal Thermal Energy Storage) to heat up the canopy 
ground area within the cold season. Additionally, integration of PV´s within the canopy roof are 
suggested. The concept states a 50 % reduction in energy consumption.  
 
Urban gardening and an aquaponic system are suggested along with sustainable ideas of waste 
treatment, collection of rainwater and intended educational qualities. The rendering at Figure 1-1 is 
the front page of the competition proposal, showing the green luxuriant space within the chanterelle  
formed EKO-Canopy.  
 
Figure 1-1 Front page of EKO-Canopy competition proposal by White Architects, Stockholm (White 2015) 
The EKO-Canopy project is a concept idea by White Architects prepared as a proposal for a 
Swedish competition delivered in September 2015 with following objective:  
“Innovative utilisation of excess heat in future cities 
The competition deals with how we, with help of innovative solutions of utilizing low-temperature waste heat, 
can make our cities a more satisfying and sustainable place to live in. Low temperature excess heat refers to 
water with temperatures between 30 to 50 °C." (Restvärmekonsortiet et al. 2015) (Translated from Swedish) 
 
“Excess heat refers to heat from various industrial processes. Excess heat with higher temperatures are widely 
used, for example for district heating. But large amounts of the excess heat is not utilized.” 
(Restvärmekonsortiet et al. 2015) (Translated from Swedish)

1.2 
Nordic Built 
The thesis is supported by Nordic Built, which is an initiative by the Nordic built environment to 
collaborate with a goal of accelerating and developing sustainable building concepts. Nordic Built is 
based on the following ten principles:  
Nordic Built principles: 
1. Is made for people and promotes quality of life 
2. Pushes the limits of sustainable performance, as a result of our innovative mind-set 
and high level of knowledge 
3. Merges urban living with the qualities of nature 
4. Achieves zero emissions over its lifecycle 
5. Is functional, smart and aesthetically appealing, building on the best of the Nordic 
design tradition 
6. Is robust, durable, flexible and timeless - built to last 
7. Utilises local resources and is adapted to local conditions 
8. Is produced and maintained through partnerships founded on transparent 
collaboration across borders and disciplines. 
9. Employs concepts that are scalable and used globally 
10. Profits people, business and the environment 
Source: (Innovation 2013)  
.  
==PAGE:13==
1.3 - Introduction 
Page 11 
 
1.3 
Problem statement 
With respect to the Nordic Built principles and competition feedback on the EKO-Canopy 
proposal by White Architects: What measures can be taken to improve energy performance of 
Miljonprogrammet apartment buildings by adding a transparent canopy covering between the 
existing buildings, without compromising on adequate indoor climate in both apartments and within 
the canopy? 
Is there an alternative to the proposed glass construction that can help to solve the questionable 
aspects from the competition jury feedback, that takes into account economy and environmental 
footprint?  
How does alternative geometries compare regarding thermal performance, snow management and 
critical wind loads? 
How will different types of EKO-Canopy solutions affect daylight conditions within existing 
apartments? Can the measures help to design a solution that include acceptable daylight conditions? 
Does application of an EKO-Canopy make basis for integrated strategies that can reduce energy 
consumption of the existing apartment buildings and at the same time improve the indoor climate 
within the canopy? 
 
==PAGE:14==
1.4 - Introduction 
Page 12 
 
1.4 
Background 
1.4.1 
Miljonprogrammet  
The Dragonvägen buildings from 1971 are categorised as a part of “Miljonprogrammet” (English: 
Swedish Million Homes Program). From 1965 to 1974 an ambiguous public housing program was 
enrolled to accommodate housing shortage in Sweden due to increased urbanisation. 
Miljonprogrammet included construction of more than a million dwellings. The apartments from 
this period were mainly build as pre-cast concrete building blocks with 2-7 stories. The social 
housing blocks are similar to what is found from the same period around in many European 
countries. (Shafqat 2011) 
The residential buildings was built during years with cheap oil prices whereas only small amounts of 
insulation was installed, resulting in poor insulating performance of the buildings from this time. 
Many of the buildings are in poor condition due to lack of maintenance. (Hall & Vidén 2005) 
Buildings from the Miljonprogrammet are in general characterized by a high degree of uniformity 
and strict planning. Many people want to move from the Miljonprogrammet houses as the areas are 
often characterized by social deprivation but most people living within the Miljonprogrammet has 
low income and can´t afford to move. However, many people are living within Miljonprogrammet 
housing and renovations are being performed improving both the architectural quality, and the 
energy performance of the buildings. (Presentations at SIRen meeting 4. December 2015 by Jan 
Bröchner, Chalmers, Hans Lind and Kristina Mjörnell)  
The largest share of buildings from Miljonprogrammet are 1-3-stories in height. About 20 % are 
buildings blocks with 6-8 floors as the Dragonvägen buildings. A figure showing distribution of 
building block heights of Miljonprogrammet housing is shown in appendixxx. 
 
 
Figure 1-2 Sollentuna apartment buildings from Miljonprogrammet (Source: aftonbladet.se) 
==PAGE:15==
1.4 - Introduction 
Page 13 
 
1.4.2 
Dragonvägen - case study  
This section is mainly based on a survey checklist on Dragonvägen 2-10 and Dragonvägen 12-20  
completed by BeBos (Ab & Ritningar 2013). 
Dragonvägen residential building blocks are chosen as case study for the thesis investigations. The 
buildings are typical Miljonprogram 8 stories residential buildings consisting of eight similar 
apartment blocks as seen at Figure 1-3. Building surveys has been done during 2013 as the buildings 
are about to go through an extensive renovation. The two buildings to the right at Figure 1-3 are the 
surveyed buildings currently being renovated. Studies are performed for the similar Dragonvägen 
buildings marked with green, where buildings are positioned on both sides with same distance of 30 
m in between.  
 
Figure 1-3 Dragonvägen apartment buildings. The surveyed buildings are named at the right of the picture. 
The case study buildings are marked with a green dashed square at the left. Dragonvägen is located in 
Upplands Vasby, about 20 km NNW of Stockholm city, Sweden. (Source: Google Earth) 
Apartment floors are identical, see plan drawing at Figure 1-4. (See appendixxx for larger plan 
drawing). The length of the building blocks is 72.56 m and the depth is 12.0 m and 13.75 m with the 
south oriented balconies included. Without including the 2.53 m penthouse, the building is 22.6 m 
high at the east end and 19.7 at the west end as the ground rises from one end to the other. The 
buildings are the highest in the urban area, therefore wind exposure should be considered 
accordingly, with building 1 being the most exposed. A room scheme of the apartment floors is 
shown at Table 1-1 below.

The Dragonvägen apartment buildings ware 
build in 1971 and are now owned by AB 
Väsbyhem. The buildings include 8 floors and a 
penthouse/attic which include elevator shafts, 
ventilation rooms and two small apartments. 
The netto floor area of the Dragonvägen 
buildings are found to be 6700 m2 and 5183 m2 
without external walls. 
No apartments are located at the ground floor, 
which include bicycle rooms, common facilities, 
storage and washing room, garbage room, as 
well as installations for district heating and 
more. The ground floor is heated to 18°C, 
partly by excess heat from installations. 
 
Figure 1-5 Dragonvägen north facade 
Each apartment floor is 2.70 m with internal 
room heights of 2.45 m. Height of ground floor 
is 3.2 m.  
Glazing shutters have been applied to the 
balconies at the south façade as a part of an 
energy renovation. Some apartments do not 
include the glazing.  
Asbestos cement boards are used at the internal 
part of the Dragonvägen external walls. 
Asbestos is found in many Miljonprogram 
building blocks, making renovation of facades 
complicated and prolongs renovation time 
(Asbestforening 2010). Further issues are due 
when occupants have to be accommodated for 
longer periods. 
 
Figure 1-6 Dragonvägen south façade  
Recent improvements in 2010 resulted in 24% energy reduction from 177 to 134 kWh/m2. 
Improvements included new hot water circulation pumps, adjustment of heating system and 
replacement of thermostatic valves, new heat exchanger for district heating and new pressure 
controlled fans and installation of kitchen hoods. 
No individual measuring equipment is installed for each apartment. This is considered to increase 
the heating consumption, as occupants do not pay directly for what they use. Therefore installation 
of individual measure equipment is planned. To this also come problems with corrosion of heating 
pipes located in the stair case shafts

An assessment with thermal photography has been performed on Dragonvägen 10. Further 
description and selected images are shown in appendixxx, where also reference pictures are 
included. Results show high heat losses from thermal bridges at window frames and wall/ceiling 
joints. Particularly high heat losses are found at most balcony doors and several windows, indicating 
air leakages. Thermal leakage is further processed in (PART 3:5.2.1.1 - IESVE existing conditions) 
1.4.2.1 
Blower door tests have been performed on the same apartments. Test results have 
been calculated to be corresponding with 0.86 l/s and 1.20 l/s per floor area for 
the two investigated apartments. The infiltration is further processed in (PART 
3:5.3.1.5 - Natural ventilation 
Natural ventilation within residential buildings is difficult to predict. A simplification of natural 
ventilation within the apartments are selected. An air change of 5 ACH is setup with a ramp 
function from 23.5 °C to 27 °C where the ventilation will each 5 ACH.  
Natural ventilation within apartments are modelled with a simple 
ramp function with gradually opening of windows for natural 
ventilation

1.4 - Introduction

1.4.3 
Renovation – energy and costs 
The building sector in European countries constitutes about 40 % of the total energy consumption 
and about the same share in CO2 emissions (Economidou et al. 2011). The same is assumed for 
Nordic countries (Shafqat 2011). Reports from all over the world show that global warming is a 
major intensifying threat to humans as well as animals and plants. Rising sea levels, drought and 
extreme weather are some of the consequences of global warming (Source: eea.europa.eu).  
As the building stock only increases with about 1 % a year, the existing building stock will be the 
major contributor of CO2 emissions for many years, making energy renovation of the existing 
building stock a critically essential way to reduce the global warming. About 75 % of the buildings 
within Europe are residential buildings, which makes energy renovation of these highly important. 
(Economidou et al. 2011) 
Halvera Mera is the Swedish goal of cutting the energy consumption from the building industry by 
2050 by 50 % and reduce GHG (Green House Gas) emission to net-zero. The building stock is seen 
as a profitable way to reach the goals of carbon neutrality (Woodruff & Mankoff 2015).  
Participation in a workshop with SIRen (Sustainable Integrated Ren-ovation) the 4. December 2015 
has been a part of the thesis research.  
The program can be found in appendixxxx . The attendance included a presentation of the recent 
thesis investigations of the EKO-Canopy project and attained information about the newest 
research within the Swedish built environment. SIRen is a Swedish inter-disciplinary research 
environment for holistic sustainable renovation. The organisation is driven by researchers from 
universities and business collaborators (renoveringscentrum.lth.se) 
Table 1-4 and Table 1-5 show results acquired from a SIRen presentation by Mikael Mangold, 
Magnus Österbring, Holger Wallbaum, Paula Femenias, Liane Thuvander “Socio-economic impact 
of renovation and energy retrofitting of the Gothenburg multi-family dwellings”  
(Mangold et al. 2014) 
Table 1-4 shows information about six “State-of-the-art” Swedish renovation projects. The average 
annual energy consumption before renovations are shown to be 184 kWh/m2. This is significantly 
higher than the energy consumption of Dragonvägen of 133 kWh/m2 which makes sense as recent 
energy improvements has been completed on the Dragonvägen buildings. The state-of-the-art 
renovations reduces the annual energy consumption to 74 kWh/m2 which compare to an average 
reduction of 60 % compared to previous energy consumption.

Table 1-4 also show costs for the renovation, including costs used for energy improvements. On

Energy consumption both includes heating of buildings as well as electricity consumption for 
ventilation, pumps and lighting of common spaces etc. AB Vasbyhem has a goal of reducing the 
total annual energy consumption to 75 kWh/m2 for existing buildings. This correspond well with 
the average energy consumption 74 kWh/m2 from Table 1-4 and compares to AB Vasbyhems new 
buildings, where about 15 kWh/m2 is used for electricity for lighting, fan power and pumps and 
about 60 kWh/m2 for heating and DHW.  
AB Vasbyhem seeks to lower energy consumption for DHW by installing individual measuring units 
in existing apartments. A system that recover heat from waste water is being developed in 
cooperation with Stockholmshem and KTH.  
AB Vasbyhem has planned the ventilation system of Dragonvägen to be a FX-system with heat 
recovery of exhaust air via a heat pump to the heating system or alternatively DHW water.

1.4.4 
Renovation – social and environmental aspects 
There are many views on how to deal with the deprived Miljonprogrammet building stock. The 
Swedish research council, Formas has prepared the report “Miljonprogrammet – Develop or 
demolish” (Formas et al. 2012) in which many aspects of how to deal with the Miljonprogrammet 
housing is discussed by several researches. As the title indicate, there are considerations of 
demolishing the buildings and build up whole new areas as well as considerations of how to 
refurbish the existing building stock. Both approaches incorporate goals of improving both the 
social aspects as well as energy efficiency. - The report includes the concept of urban gardening as a 
concept with many positive outcomes. (Formas et al. 2012) 
However, the report states that comprehensive renovation and refurbishment should be the main 
solution for the 600 thousand of the total of about 830 thousand apartments from the 
Miljonprogrammet. The Miljonprogrammet apartment buildings are built robust and allow for 
flexibility. About 200 thousand apartments have been modernised, mainly due to governmental 
subsidies. Additional insulation and new windows have only been installed in about ¼ of the 
modernisations. An overview of the modernisations is shown in Figure 1-7. 
 
Figure 1-7 Refurbishments, Miljonprogramme apartments 1975-2011 (Formas et al. 2012) (page 42, translated) 
 appendixxx. (Formas et al. 2012)  
A socially sustainable renovation includes that occupants can stay in the area during a renovation 
process. Furthermore, social sustainable renovation also takes into account that low income 
households can afford to stay after refurbishment. (Lind et al. 2014) Numbers for average increased 
monthly rentals are shown in the last row in Table 1-5. (Formas et al. 2012) recommend subsides for 
renovations of Miljonprogrammet housing to ensure socially sustainable renovations. The EKO-
Canopy project could potentially be supported by the government or the municipality to support 
urban development. 
“GHG emissions for Miljonprogrammet housing are lower than the average emission of GHG” 
(Woodruff & Mankoff 2015)  
 “Future studies on retrofitting of multi-family-buildings for the purpose of reducing GHG emission 
need to have a component of social justice” (Mangold et al. 2014) 
==PAGE:21==
1.1 - Introduction 
Page 19 
 
 METHOD 
PART 2:
1.1 
Integrated, iterative design process 
Investigation of 3rd room spaces has been investigated for many years and has received increasingly 
more focus with the recent years increased focus on sustainability and green energy. 
This report seeks to investigate the wide perspectives of the EKO-Canopy concept in order to 
speed up the process of a potential realisation. By approaching the project with a holistic integrated 
design process it is the goal to bring up as many important technical aspects as possible, that can 
guide the project in its initial design phase.  
This report includes investigations of the EKO-Canopy renovation concept and development of the 
concept into an actual design. The goal of the investigations, is to examine whether the EKO-
Canopy concept can compete with the conventional renovation strategies on both energy 
performance and LCA.  
Enrolment of the EKO-Canopy concept, could potentially result in energy savings and a positive 
environmental impact for Miljonprogrammet buildings, as well as improve the urban development. 
As the concept is still in its initial phase, a variety of critical challenges must be solved before a 
potential pilot project is possible. The challenges involve: reduced daylight in apartments, structural 
design, snow management, wind loads, fire safety, selection of materials, ventilation strategy etc.  
Investigations of possible solutions taking into account these critical challenges have been 
performed in this project, using and integrated design approach; including as many design 
parameters as possible which will influence performance of the EKO-Canopy. The design approach 
is illustrated in Figure 2-1, with factors taken into account.  
 
Figure 2-1 Illustration of integrated design with an iterative process. The spiral both demonstrates the 
parameters to be included in the integrated design process as well as the iterative process. Each parameter is 
taken into account several times during the investigations as illustrated by the spiral.  
==PAGE:22==
1.2 - Introduction

1.2 
Thesis study methods  
The thesis is based on various study methods, including research, computer simulations, 
calculations, study trips and presentations and feedback from supervisors. An overview is shown in 
the diagram at Figure 2-2. 
 
 
Figure 2-2 Diagram showing overview of thesis study methods 
The wide range of research studies seeks to concentrate the more time consuming investigations on 
subjects that has interest in the EKO-Canopy concept. As the project is considered for a potential 
realisation, critical reviews from a wide range of professionals have been shaping the focus areas 
during the thesis.  
==PAGE:23==
1.3 - Introduction 
Page 21 
 
1.3 
Software 
1.3.1 
Simulation software  
1.3.1.1 
Velux Daylight Visualizer 
Daylight conditions are investigated using Velux Daylight Visualizer (VDV) simulation software. 
The software allows for easy import of building geometry modelled in various CAD formats, it is 
fast and allows for rather easy investigation of daylight conditions. Since the output files size is 
small, compared to Daysim (also freely available), VDV was chosen in consideration to the extent of 
daylight simulations performed in this project. The software is provided for free by Velux.  
1.3.1.2 
IES-Virtual Environment 
IES-VE is one of the leading tool for dynamic simulation of indoor climate an energy consumption 
in building projects. The software allows for import of different CAD formats; for this project the 
geometry was imported directly from SketchUp using the dedicated VE for SketchUp plugin. 
Energy consumption, indoor climate, solar potential, natural and mechanical ventilation are 
investigated with IES-VE dynamic simulation software.  
1.3.1.3 
Autodesk Flow Design 
Wind pressure and wind velocity is investigated with Autodesk Flow Design. The CFD software 
uses Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) equations to calculate steady state air flows around 
buildings. 
1.3.2 
Other software  
• 
Mendeley for bibliography  
• 
MS Excel has been used for various tasks during the thesis project, including: 
o Processing and presentation of simulation output data 
o Calculation of simulation input data 
o Quick calculations for conceptual calculations  
o Calculation of surface areas, volume and quantities  
o Grid point files for VDV (Including WordPad) 
• 
SketchUp 
o IES-VE plugin/extension: Modelling of geometry for import to IES-VE  
o Geometry modelling for VDV 
o Geometry for generating OBJ files for Flow Design  
o Geometry modelling for form finding, areas and visual assessment 
o Figures and diagrams 
o Extension/plugin: Minimal surfaces by Josef Leibinger tensile-structures.de 
o Extension/plugin: s4u-Frame (Uses lines to generate frame structures) 
 
Simulations and work has been performed on  Macbook Pro Retina Mid 2012 with OSX 10.9.5

 RESULTS 
PART 3:
The results part includes the main investigations in the thesis. This include “Preliminary 
investigations”, parameter studies of the canopy´s influence on “Daylight”, “Canopy geometry 
investigations” regarding surface area, snow handling and simulations of wind loads. This is 
followed by investigations of “Energy performance and indoor climate” for both the existing 
apartments as well as the canopy. Finally, the investigations are considered with estimations of costs 
and environmental impacts. Partial conclusions and discussion is addressed along with the 
investigations. Overall “Conclusion summary” and Discussion is addressed in (PART 4:) and 
(PART 5:) 
2 
Preliminary investigations 
This section includes relevant research studies. This includes a series of reference projects that can 
inspire the design of the EKO-Canopy and research of various transparent building materials for the 
canopy envelope. Furthermore, basic issues about fire are addressed followed by relevant acquired 
weather data and preliminary considerations about snow management.  
2.1 
EKO-Canopy reference projects 
The EKO-Canopy project is still in an early design phase. The structure, the form, the energy 
systems, the materials, the function and the performance is still unknown. Research has been done 
in order to find inspiration from the built environment, where many canopy-alike buildings have 
been designed, been built and been in service. The research studies both include study trips as well 
as literature studies. 
2.1.1 
Bovieran – Swedish housing concept 
Bovieran is a newly introduced Swedish building 
concept for seniors from +55 years. The concept 
includes a U-formed 3-storey apartment building 
with common courtyard covered in glass with 
common areas and tropical climate.  
The building concept is becoming increasingly 
more popular in Sweden. About 20 projects have 
already been built and 10 more are on its way. 
Bovieran projects are similar and take less than a 
year to build. 
 
Figure 3-1 Bovieran U-shaped plan (Source: 
svenskfast.se) 
 
Figure 3-2 Bovieran housing (Source: svenskfast.se) 
 
Figure 3-3 Bovieran projects (Source: bovieran.se 
texts is translated and added to the figure) 
==PAGE:25==
2.1 - Preliminary investigations 
Page 23 
 
A study trip to Riksbyggen Bovieran Västerhaninge, outside Stockholm was completed on October 
28th. The trip included a meeting with presentation of the Bovieran housing concept by the 
Technical Manager, Peter Kranz. The meeting was held at a booked table within the glass covered 
atrium garden, founding the courtyard of the senior housings. After the presentation, questions were 
answered and discussed among the participants, giving additional detailed information about how 
the building functions. Among others, meeting participants included myself and: 
• 
Peter Kranz, Technical Manager at Bovieran Västerhaninge 
• 
Elise Grosse, Architect in the environmental department at White Architects 
• 
Thomas Sunden, Head of Sustainable Building Issues at Sustainable Innovation  
• 
Olof Sjöberg, Sustainability Manager AB Stockholmshem 
After the meeting a guided tour around in the building allowed a closer look on the construction, 
the garden, apartments as well as the technical room. Documentation was made with photos, 
installed thermostats and hygrometers as well as a Lux-meter.  
 
    
 
As time was short, thorough daylight investigations 
could not be performed. However, without proper 
setup, the indoor illuminance was measured to be 
3024 lux and the outdoor illuminance to 7836 lux, 
resulting in a daylight factor of 39 %DF (for more 
information, see (4.2.1 - Daylight Factor (DF)). 
The indoor spot was measured within the green 
circle at Figure 3-4. The measurements should be 
considered a high degree of uncertainty as the they 
were performed with changing cloud cover and time 
interval in between. However the measurement can 
give an indication about the relatively high level of 
daylight inside the glass covered garden. 
 
 
Figure 3-4 Photo of Bovieran courtyard with glass roof covering. The courtyard is decorated with tropical 
plants and light pathways with occasional spaces around including tables, benches and possible recreation 
activities. Wide access ways to apartments are located along the buildings. Meeting was held at the table 
marked with green. 
The space was relatively empty during the visit on a Wednesday morning. However, Peter Kranz 
informed that the garden is diligently used by the occupants in general including families and friends 
==PAGE:26==
2.1 - Preliminary investigations 
Page 24 
 
giving large social benefits. Although the space was well lit and the acoustics seemed fine, both 
daylight and acoustics was considered to be different than outdoors. Many of the occupants where 
seniors who had earlier been living on the countryside.  
The night prior to the morning meeting was 2 °C with a clear sky with frost on the grass. At around 
10 am temperatures within the atrium was measured to be 16.4 °C at ground level in the middle of 
the courtyard with a corresponding temperature at 2nd floor. The temperature usually reach 20°C at 
mid-day in October. The lowest recorded temperatures measured in Bovieran was informed to be 
around 10°C. No clear information about highest temperatures was given, but temperatures ware 
sometimes too high within apartments. 
The atrium is constructed with double layer insulating glass. Safety glass is required as thick safety 
glass allows to walk on the roof. The large glazing surfaces are a considerable maintenance factor, 
for that reason Bovieran projects are located outside of cities to avoid dirt from traffic.  
The Bovieran glass roof is carried by a steel truss construction with crossing frames forming the 
roof. With a span of 32 m and a heavy safety glass roof, it is assumed that thorough optimization of 
the roof has been performed in order to achieve the light appearance. The truss beams are 
positioned at every 4.5 m.  
 
Figure 3-5 Bovieran roof construction. Steel truss system with a span of 32 m including a lateral faming system 
holding the double layer glazing. Pink circles show positioning of ceiling fans, which are positioned series of 3 
on every second truss beam. Windows with mechanical openings are located on each side of the roof ridge. 
Windows with mechanical openings are positioned along the roof ridge. Four windows of 
approximately 1.5 m2 each are located between each truss beam, with additional windows located 
along the roof/wall attachment, resulting in a total roof opening area of about 70-80 m2. The glass 
gable wall includes vertical window openings of the same type with a total estimated area of about 
50 m2.  
Single motors control series of windows using a gearing mechanism with threaded steel rods – a 
system similar to what is found in the green house industry, see (2.1.3 - Green houses). 
Three ceiling fans are distributed at the glass roof on every second truss beam, resulting in 12 ceiling 
fans all together, see pink circles at Figure 3-5. Ventilation within the atrium is mainly supplied with 
diffusers directing the air at the glass roof, (see pink markings at Figure 3-6). Placement of diffusers 
==PAGE:27==
2.1 - Preliminary investigations 
Page 25 
 
can be explained by the needs of avoiding condensation on the glass. Further more, the diffusers are 
used to melt snow on the roof in wintertime. Ceiling fans mixes the supply air and equals out the 
temperatures within the atrium space.  
 
Figure 3-6 Bovieran roof connection. Supply air ducts (Red markings) are placed along the roof/wall assembly 
with air diffusers directing the air up at the glass roof. Several window openings are controlled by single 
motors using a gearing mechanism with a threaded steel rods (Green circles).  
A large centralized ventilation system with rotating heat recovery and moisture control is used for 
the atrium. The RH (Relative Humidity) is considered to be a problem at times which is both 
experienced as “heavy humid air” and is also seen as condensation on the windows (which was also 
the case the morning of the visit). Humidity is normally kept between 60-80 %RH. At the time of 
the visit, 77 %RH was found at the atrium ground level and 64 %RH was found at the 3rd floor. 
A separate ventilation aggregate is used for the apartments for which dehumidification is needed at 
times where humidity levels are too high. A “moisture shell” has been applied within the walls facing 
the atrium. The apartments are heated by ventilation air - and radiators when needed.  
An open fishpond of about 40 m2 is located at the shaded side of the courtyard which is also serving 
as humidifier along with the plants. The canopy garden is decorated with large stones which are 
assumed to be contributing as thermal mass within the atrium space.  
No sprinkler system is added within the atrium as fire regulations consider the space as outdoors. 
However a warning system is installed similar to those found within shopping malls.  
The price range is about 200.000 € for an apartment of 87m2 with a monthly rent of about 530 €.

2.1 - Preliminary investigations

2.1.2 
Canopy projects 
Reference Projects 
Reference Picture 
The Crystal Palace (1851) has to be mentioned as a 
historical icon of a largescale transparent construction. It 
was a cast iron construction with a height of 39 meters, only 
possible by utilizing the new developed method of “cast 
plate glass”. It was built to house the Great Exhibition 
showing new technology from the industrial revolution in 
1851. 
The building was destroyed by fire during 2nd World War 
but is still standing as an icon. (Source: Wikipedia.org) 
 
Dome of Visions (DoV) is an ongoing project by DTU, 
Aalborg University KTH and NCC, investigating various 
aspects of the “3rd space” and the idea of having a house 
inside a greenhouse. The first DoV was erected march 2013 
in Copenhagen (photo on the right) and a DoV 2.0 has just 
been erected in Stockholm, Sweden (photo below). A DoV 
version 3.0 is due this summer in Aarhus, DK. 
The dome envelope consists of one layer acrylic plates with 
poor insulating properties. Assembling’s are relatively leaky 
with about 1 ACH. The temperature inside the dome is 
measured to be about 4 °C higher on average than outdoor 
temperature. However, heating is applied directly in the semi 
outdoor space and heat losses from internal house 
contributed considerably to the temperature (Papachristou & 
Foteinaki 2015) 
The dome protects against wind and rain and is considered 
to be a very comfortable and popular space including a café 
and lots of plants. The wood construction contributes to the 
appealing atmosphere, attracting countless of events of all 
kind. (Sources domeofvisions.dk & domeofvisions.se) 
Link: Semi-outdoor space, thermal indoor climate, energy 
 
 
 
EMBRACE Solar Decathlon house – developed and 
build by Team DTU for the European Solar Decathlon 
competition in Versailles 2014 with assistance and support 
by companies from the building industry.  
The house is designed to be placed on top of existing 
buildings, as an alternative to conventional roof renovation. 
The house is a plus-energy-house that produces more energy 
than it consumes over a year. PV´s are integrated in the glass 
covering establishing the roof of a sheltered garden, 
intended to be functioning as a passively heated space. Many 
other Solar Decathlon houses were playing with similar 
integrated semi-outdoor spaces, growing editable plants 
based on ideas of self-sufficiency. 
Requirements of thick safety glass for roof covering resulted 
in high costs and negative effect on the LCA.  
Link: Integration of PV´s, Semi-outdoor space, integration with 
existing buildings, energy efficiency   
 
 
==PAGE:29==
2.1 - Preliminary investigations

2.1.3 
Green houses 
Reference Projects 
Reference Picture 
Naturhuset in Sikhall located 20 km north of Trollhättan, 
Sweden, is another example of a house inside a greenhouse. 
The house is 145 m2 and the transparent envelope is a 
traditional greenhouse of 300 m2 with one-layer glass and 
large automatically controlled ventilation openings in the 
roof.  
The greenhouse installation is estimated to reduce energy 
consumption by 50 %. Fresh air is supplied through a duct 
in the ground stabilising the air inlet temperature. Although, 
the greenhouse can reach 40 °C on hot days. The timber 
house works as thermal mass improving inside temperatures. 
No mould or moisture problems have been found at the 
timber house construction. The efficient ventilation 
openings allow hot humid air to be ventilated. Rainwater is 
filtrated and used for watering vegetables, fruit trees and 
plants that additionally help to purify the air and stabilize 
humidity. Still, too dry air occurs occasionally. 
Waste treatment systems are well integrated, resulting in an 
overall minimal footprint during the buildings operation. 
(Aune & Solvarm 2014) (Solvarm et al. 2014) 
Link: Passive solar energy, ventilation, humidity, self sufficiency 
 
 
Tomato greenhouse, Netherlands 
As a part of a student trip during a course in Smart Skin at 
TU München in 2015, I had the change to visit a newly 
established largescale tomato greenhouse measuring 250m x 
150m, using the newest technologies within the green house 
industry. The indoor climate was kept at very decent 
temperatures all year around thanks to an efficient 
mechanically controlled natural ventilation system in the 
roof and large scale aggregates for heating and cooling. 
Although solar reflective paint was applied at the roof, the 
space still flooded with an appealing diffuse light. 
Link: Systems, natural ventilation, solar control, greenery  
 
Polycarbonate greenhouses – are popular for the lightness 
of the material. This combined with the larger sheet sizes 
(than what is found for glass) can reduce construction costs. 
Polycarbonate has also been used in architecture, including: 
Aviva football stadium(Dublin, Ireland), Amsterdam Arena, 
Prismen in CPH. (See Appendixxx Material investigations) 
(source: (Gove 2014), EuPC Sector Group) 
Link: Polycarbonate as building material, lightweight 
 
Retractable roof greenhouses – Cravo company 
specializing in retractable roofs for greenhouses. Natural 
ventilation is assumed as an important method to avoid 
overheating in the hot season. Removing the roof or part of 
the roof could be a way to efficiently ventilate the canopy 
during hot season. (Source: cravo.com) 
Link: Natural ventilation openings

2.1.4 
ETFE constructions 
Transparent lightweight structures using insulating cushions of thin layers ETFE has been found 
during studies of reference projects. (See 2.4.4 – ETFE) for more information about the material.  
Reference Projects 
Reference Picture 
Eden Project (2001), Cornwall, UK – an example of a 
large-scale super lightweight structure using multiple layer 
cushions of ETFE. The large biomes are constructed with 
galvanized steel tubes forming hexagons and are intelligently 
reinforced with a wire system. The construction itself weigh 
less than the air inside. Eden project ground area include 
30.000 m2 of space and heights of up to 55m. A space 
covered with various floras and activities - a popular theme 
centre educating about green transformation, food 
production and more.  
Sources: (Morris 2012) (edenproject.com) 
Link: Lightweight ETFE construction, Indoor climate, Natural 
ventilation, Green transformation,  
 
Renovation of Kingsdale School (2002) – An excellent 
example of how architecture can help change a struggled 
social environment. The school have had poor reputation, 
low grades influenced by non-attendance. Initiatives 
including a new head teacher and a comprehensive 
refurbishment of the school has been changing the school 
into a popular school with good grades and “Outstanding 
Behaviour and Safety of pupils” (Ofsted School Report 
2012) The refurbishment included ETFE roof covering of 
the central courtyard establishing a new meeting space. The 
lightweight ETFE roof spans 40m x 80m = 3200 m2, with a 
weight of only 25kg/m2. The construction price was 2.3 m. €  
when constructed in 2002. This included an intelligent 
shading control mechanism of the inflated cushions. See 
picture to the right. (Source: (Morrison et al. 2004)) 
 
Link: Social transformation, Lightweight, Solar shading, costs 
 
 
Water Cube, Olympic Swimming Centre, Beijing (2008) 
- A glowing example of how ETFE and LED lighting goes 
together. Natural ventilation within the envelope space 
frame structure reduces cooling needs significantly along 
with the internal thermal mass (water & surfaces). Air supply 
at the internal walls is supplied to avoid water condensation 
on the internal surfaces during cold season. 
(Source: architectureau.com) 
Link: Artificial lighting, Moisture regulation, water, thermal mass 
 
 
==PAGE:31==
2.1 - Preliminary investigations 
Page 29 
 
Alliance Arena, Munich (2005) – Another example of a 
prestige project using ETFE cushions. The ETFE has a 
dotted surface, which appears white from outside when 
LED´s are turned off. The form of the ETFE cushions has 
shown to allow snow to collect between the cushions, which 
require huge expenses for removing snow during winter also 
caused by the large roof surface. Pressure in cushions is 
monitored and controlled according to snow loads. 
(Source: allianz-arena.de) 
Link: Snow management, dotted ETFE, pressure monitoring 
 
Waitomo Glowworm Caves (2012), visitor centre, New 
Zealand A timber/wire construction with ETFE cushions 
attached on thin steel frames with integrated gutters. An 
elegant arching toroid, formed by prefabricated I-beams 
made of LVL (local) pine wood, interwoven and hold 
together by clamps, resulting in long uninterrupted ETFE 
cushions with a width of 4-5m.  
(Source: Wellington company & Vector Foiltec) 
Link: Wire/wood/steel construction, Local wood, LCA 
 
Khan Shatyr Entertainment Center (2006) - The largest 
tent in the world with a height of 150 m and an elliptical 
base covering an area of 140 thousand m2. Located in 
Kazakhstan, where outdoor temperatures vary from -35°C 
to +35 °C. However, the climate inside is conditioned to 
more human temperatures between +15-30 °C. Internal 
water pools are enclosed to avoid high humidity.  
The construction is based on a centred tripod column with 
cable wires hanging from the top, forming the tent surface 
of insulating ETFE cushions.  
Sources: (Lane 2010) (Cossio et al. 2012) (Vector Foiltec 
2014)  
Link: Wire construction, insulating ETFE cushions, Indoor climate 
 
 
A guest lecture at DTU, 1. December 2015 by William 
F. Bakers – An American structural engineer known 
for engineering the tallest building in the world, Burj 
Khalifa - gave inspiration to work with alternative 
adaptable geometries. The lecture “On the Harmony of 
Theory and Practice in the Design of Tall Buildings” 
presented interesting ways of optimizing shell 
structures using optimizing software. The lecture can 
be found online.

2.2 
Considerations of fire risks and management  
When a residential environment is changed drastically as the EKO-Canopy with the new enclosed 
space, fire risks have to be taken into account. Areas that currently facing outside are changed to a 
sheltered space. Although the space can be considered as an outdoor space in some aspects, there 
are situations regarding fire management that has to be considered.  
Currently the balconies at Dragonvägen are considered as temporary escape ways for apartments. If 
smoke is trapped within the canopy, the balconies will no longer function as escape ways. Therefore 
efficient smoke ventilation is considered to be required. No calculations have been performed to 
dimension the size of such smoke vents, or if forced smoke vents are necessary. Natural ventilation 
openings are investigated within (5.4.2.3 - Natural ventilation in hot season) 
Ventilation openings should be able to function at all time of the year. If openings for natural 
ventilations are included as a part of the smoke ventilation strategy, well-functioning openings in 
cases with snow and frost should be considered although the natural ventilation is not used during 
this time of year. Blocking of escape ways in winter should also be considered when management of 
snow from the canopy construction is addressed.  
If the canopy space allow many people to be present at the same time, escape ways should be 
dimensioned large enough and with distances that allow people to escape in time. Various scenarios 
should be considered. 
The existing conditions only allows exit from stair cases at the south side of the building below the 
balconies. A solution has to be found for the building with exists facing the canopy. One solution 
could be new passageways through the stair-cases at the ground floor. However, this should be 
considered according to costs and the use of the building. 
 
Figure 3-7 A possible fire exit plan for the canopy. New passages in the buildings are shown with arrows. 
Fire safety also plays a role for the construction that has to be able to withstand fire until evacuation 
and allow access for fire fighting. This should be taken into account for the construction materials, 
where also the level of combustion is a factor to be considered along with risks about spread of fire. 
==PAGE:33==
2.3 - Preliminary investigations 
Page 31 
 
Chemical properties of materials should also be considered to avoid spread of toxic gas in risk of 
fire. The considerations of fire risks are treated within (2.4 - Canopy material research) 
The canopy space is likely to be seen as Category 3 regarding the Danish Fire Legislation BR10 §5.4. 
In this case following active approaches are needed: 
• 
Automatic Fire Detection 
• 
Automatic Sprinkler System if floor area > 2000m2 (Same for British Standard BS 
5839-6:2004 New Swedish requirements for sprinkler systems (SBF 120:7) are due 
later in 2016, source brandskyddsforeningen.se 
• 
Escape and panic lighting 
• 
Hose Reels 
2.3 
Heat transfer and solar radiation 
The thermal performance of the canopy is important to maintain desired temperatures.  
Heat loss through a construction is defined by the U-value expressed with [W/m²K], describing the 
heat loss per square meter construction per temperature difference between the two sides of the 
construction (indoor and outdoor) measured in Kelvin. A low U-value secure lower heat transfer.  
Thermal conductivity is basically the conduction of vibrations between molecules and is expressed 
with [W/mK] describing the heat transfer per meter thickness per temperature difference in 
Kelvin at each side of the material.  
Surface resistance is in general included when calculating U-values for buildings. The surface 
resistance [mK/W] describes the steady state heat transfer between the air and the material. Higher 
wind speeds increase the convective heat transfer between the air and material resulting in higher U-
values. Surface resistance is further described in (5.3.1.2 - Surface thermal resistance). 
Thermal convection is caused by the movement of air (or fluids in general) and can both be driven 
by forced airflows as well as thermal convection caused by the expansion rate of air at different 
temperatures. Convection is in general the dominating heat transfer occurring in air. The insulating 
properties of transparent construction materials are mainly caused by one or more layers of air (or 
gas). Argon is often used between the layers in thermal insulating glass, mainly due to the lower rate 
of expansion caused by temperature differences, which reduce the thermal convection.  
Heat losses due to infiltration in buildings can be seen as forced convection where outside air mixes 
with the internal air.  
Thermal radiation is radiated from all surfaces with temperatures above the Absolute Zero 
(Kelvin). This occurs when collision of atoms convert the kinetic energy into electromagnetic 
radiation. Exchange of thermal radiation is constantly occurring between surfaces facing each other. 
In general (for black body surfaces), higher temperatures results in more radiated heat from a 
surface to another surface with lower temperature. Exchange of thermal radiation both occurs 
between building surfaces, human skin as well as between the ground and sky, including from the 
sun to the surface of the earth.  
 
 
 
==PAGE:34==
2.3 - Preliminary investigations 
Page 32 
 
2.3.1 
Solar radiation 
Solar radiation plays an essential role for both solar heat gains as well as daylight within the canopy. 
 
Figure 3-8 Solar Spectrum - wavelengths emitted by 
the sun and incident radiation on Earths surface. 
(Edited figure, source: algonline.org) 
Solar radiation includes a wide span of 
wavelengths where visible light is between 
380 to 780 nm which correspond to the 
wavelengths with highest intensity from 
solar radiation, see Figure 3-8. Colour scale 
according to wavelengths are shown, with 
blue colour for shorter wavelengths at the 
transition to ultra violet, and red colours for 
longer wavelengths at the transition to the 
infrared spectrum. See Figure 3-10 for light 
transmittance through transparent 
materials.  
About 43 % of the solar radiation is within 
the visible spectrum, which we see as 
“daylight”. 
A larger portion of 52 % of the solar energy is within the non-visible infrared spectrum and about 5 
% within the UV-radiation spectrum (Geologycafe 2016). The UV-light is important for growing 
various types of vegetation (Illumitex 2015). Infrared light can be a major contributor of transmitted 
heat through windows and atriums. The contribution of solar heat gains can be seen as a positive 
impact during heating periods, and be the main contributor of overheating during hot periods.  
2.3.2 
Light transmittance in materials 
Daylight is considered to be an important design parameter for an EKO-Canopy. See (4 – Daylight) 
Figure 3-9 Solar energy transmittance through 
transparent material (Source: (Pilkington 2010)) 
Daylight has to be lead through a translucent 
material in order to reach the interior of a 
building envelope. The proportion of the light 
which passes through a material is named with 
LT (light transmittance) and is commonly given 
in percentage. Another proportion of the 
radiation is reflected and a third proportion is 
absorbed and re-radiated. Material properties 
define the percentages and varies from one 
material to another. Also the material thickness, 
the material surface and the angle of incidence 
influences how light propagates.  
When several transparent layers are present, the 
same principles apply as shown in Figure 3-9, 
resulting in further reduction of transmitted 
radiation. Inclusion of several layers is the 
typical configuration for insulating transparent 
materials, 
including 
polycarbonate 
sheets, 
ETFE cushions as well as insulating glass.  
The total solar energy transmittance is commonly named g-value or SHGC (Solar Heat Gain 
Coefficient), where g-value will be used in this report.  
Different materials absorb different wavelengths of radiation, resulting in variations of transmitted 
light. This courses deviations in the colours compared to natural sunlight. The Colour Rendering 
Index (CRI) is used to indicate how well the wavelengths are maintained with rating from 1-100. 
Poor colour rendering index lead to unnatural light conditions, which is normally seen as a negative 
==PAGE:35==
2.3 - Preliminary investigations 
Page 33 
 
aspect. Material properties for selected materials can be found in (2.4.5 - Material comparison). 
Average values of light transmittance are used for daylight simulation (See 4 – Daylight) 
Light transmittance distributed on wavelengths of selected materials is shown in Figure 3-10.  
 
Figure 3-10 Light transmittance at various wavelengths (x-axis) for transparent building materials. Visible light 
is between 380-780 nm. See Figure 3-8 for the solar spectrum wavelengths. (Source: makmax) 
2.3.2.1 
IOR (Index Of Refraction) 
The IOR (Index Of Refraction) of a material specify how light propagates through the medium, 
resulting in a bending of the light. The refractive index is described in eq (3-1) 
 
Figure 3-11 Refraction in material 
! = ! !
! 
eq (3-1) 
 
n = refractive index, 
c = speed of light in vacuum   
v = speed of light in a given medium 
The distance that the light passes through is proportional with the resulting displacement of the light 
ray. With larger angles of incidence, the displacement of light will increase. See also Figure 3-17  
Refractive indexes are found in (Gooch 2011) page 617-618. IOR values for concerning materials 
are equal to or close to 1.5 and can be found in (2.4.5 - Material comparison ).  
==PAGE:36==
2.4 - Preliminary investigations 
Page 34 
 
2.3.2.2 
Dirt influence on transparency 
 
 
Figure 3-12 Dirt influence on transparency - Light 
transmittance by dirty double-glazed windows. (Source: “Flat 
Glass Technology” p. 42 (Persson 1969)) 
 
The transparency of clear building 
materials is often reduced due to dirt. 
The exposure level and the frequency of 
cleaning are both influencing the amount 
of dirt accumulating on a transparent 
surface as well as the tilting angle and the 
surface smoothness.  
As mentioned in (2.1.1) the Bovieran 
buildings are not located close to cities, 
as air particles from the traffic will cover 
the atrium with dirt at a too fast rate and 
require too much maintenance. 
 
2.4 
Canopy material research 
The functionality, performance, price, indoor climate and architectural expression of an EKO-
Canopy covering are highly influenced by the chosen materials and designed structure. As an 
essential functionality of the canopy is to make a semi outdoor space, light transmittance and optical 
transparency is a basic requirement of the canopy covering. The following sections include theory 
about light transmittance in materials and material durability. Three selected building materials has 
been investigated and addressed, finishing with a comparison overview Table 3-1.  
2.4.1 
Material durability and strength 
Durability of materials is important during a buildings service time. Resistance to all kind of 
precipitation, temperature variations with frost and intensive UV-light are all criteria that have to be 
met in order to secure a satisfactory lifetime with a minimum of maintenance. To this also comes 
resistance to loads, where wind loads and snow loads are considered to be dimensioning factors for 
large-scale lightweight structures as the EKO-Canopy.  
Furthermore, the social housing also requires the materials to be able to resist everyday usage and 
rough treatment, including risks of vandalism to some extent. In case of breaking, safety issues such 
as scattering with sharp edges has to be considered as well as possible ways to repair damages.  
Strength of materials can include both pressure and tensile strength, where the material thickness 
also plays a role on the resulting strength. However, other strength parameters also come into play 
for a canopy envelope material such as elastic and elongation properties, modulus of elasticity etc. 
The scratch resistance of a material is difficult to specify with conventional measuring units as 
several mechanical properties come into play. Basically a scratch is a permanent plastic deformation 
caused by a compressive force. To simplify the assessment of material strengths, only “impact 
strength” and “scratch resistance” is shown in the comparison. Impact strength determine short 
time forces over a larger area, e.g. wind gusts or firecrackers or a ball or a person falling into the 
material, whereas “scratch resistance” determine the resistance to sharp objects that could come in 
contact with the material. 
==PAGE:37==
2.4 - Preliminary investigations 
Page 35 
 
2.4.2 
Glass 
 
Figure 3-13 Double layer glass in frame 
(Source: grillesnglass.com) 
Glass is the most commonly used transparent 
building material. The excellent transparency and 
long-lasting resistance to weather and tear as well as 
highly developed composition with layers of 
insulating gas and varieties of coatings makes glass a 
strong competitor in choosing a transparent 
material for buildings. Application of self-cleaning 
surfaces are available for a higher price. 
However, glass is a relatively heavy building material 
which is an important factor to consider when used 
in coverings with large spans. The stiffness of the 
material requires the construction to be kept in 
place without moving, whereas joints will be leaking 
over time.  
The hard surface of glass can lead to poor acoustical 
performance with long reverberation time. 
The shattering nature of glass require lamination of the glass and cleaning of horizontal glazing 
surfaces require thicker layer of glass resulting in more expenses and heavier construction. 
Production of glass is energy intensive as high temperatures of about 1500 °C are needed, resulting 
in relatively high environmental impact. PV´s can be integrated in glass. 
Source: (Pilkington Handbook 2014) & wikipedia.org/glassproduction 
2.4.3 
Polycarbonate 
 
Figure 3-14 8-walled clear polycarbonate 
sheet (Source: Gensin) 
Polycarbonate sheets are a relatively cheap and strong 
transparent building material. Polycarbonate sheets 
come in larger sizes than what is found for glazing and 
the weight of the sheets is only about 20 % of double 
layer glass and significantly less when compared to 
thick safety glass. This can result in less material usage 
for the construction and smaller frames to allow better 
optical properties. Sheets with multiple walled hollow 
structure can reach U-values comparable with double 
layer insulating glass with argon filling. Cold curving 
of sheets is available which improve the stiffness of 
the sheets. 
 
However, additional walls result in reduced light transmittance and reduced optical performance. 
Although the index of refraction is about the same as for glass, the channel structure disturbs the 
optical performance of the material making it difficult to look through from angles. 
The organic material has shown to be receptive to dirt and algae which in general results in a poor 
look over time. Although polycarbonate is an environmentally friendly material in itself if exposed 
properly, various adhesive materials required for UV-resistivity and fire proofing, makes it a less 
environmental friendly material. Although well-developed interlocking methods are available, the 
joints are difficult to make properly air tight. 
See Appendix Part III B2  for datasheets with further material specifications.  
Source: (Gove 2014)  
==PAGE:38==
2.4 - Preliminary investigations 
Page 36 
 
2.4.4 
ETFE 
ETFE as building material was found during the thesis research process. The qualities and 
possibilities that the material offers have resulted in higher focus on the material during the thesis. A 
contact with Vector Foiltec has been established that include mail correspondences and a telephone 
meeting with Geoffrey Walters. Vector Foiltec has developed the Texlon system which is adaptable 
to various construction types and they offer engineering and manufacturing of whole solutions of 
ETFE constructions. Mail correspondences with NOWOFOL have also been a source of 
information including acquired ETFE samples for further assessment. NOWOFOL is the main 
producer of ETFE film for building purposes and deliver the films for the Texlon system.  
 
Figure 3-15 EthyleneTetraFluorEthylene 
(ETFE) molecule (Wikipedia) 
The relatively simple ETFE molecule formula, seen 
on Figure 3-15 is in family with PTFE also known as 
TEFLON. The fluorine based polymer molecule can 
be formed into thin films that are also used to contain 
various chemicals due to its excellent electrical, high-
energy radiation and chemical resistance. The stable 
molecule structure means that the material doesn´t 
degrade over time. (Cossio et al. 2012) 
 
 
Figure 3-16 Frame assembly of 3-layer ETFE 
cushion (Source: ) 
ETFE is also used in architecture (as shown in section 
2.1.4 - ETFE constructions), where the material allow 
construction of elegant and very lightweight structures 
that uses a minimum of material and allow large spans 
and flexible geometries. The ETFE film is inflated 
into cushions and fastened along the edges as seen on 
Figure 3-16. Inflation of the cushions require an 
inflation system that increases the construction costs. 
However, the energy consumption for inflation is 
found to be minimal with just 50 W per 1000m2.  
A great advantage of ETFE is its anti-adhesive properties and smooth surface that allow dirt to be 
washed off in rain. Cleaning of the EKO-Canopy was considered to be an issue from the jury review 
(see PART 1:1.1 - EKO-Canopy by White Architects).  
ETFE is normally produced by extrusion, which theoretically allow production in infinite lengths. 
However, the extrusion width is for the most productions limited to about 1.5 m, and wider 
cushions need to be welded. 
The material has been tested to be long lasting with a lifetime of about 30 years before colour 
instability, brittleness and lacking durability starts to appear. However, the scratch resistance has 
been tested on the material sample from NOWOFOL, which demonstrate that the surface is rather 
receptive to scratches and very weak against sharp materials. This show the importance of having 
the ETFE out of reach for people that could easily do damage, although the impact resistance has 
shown to be higher than for glass. In case of puncture, repair with patches can be made.  
ETFE has excellent acoustical performances as it appears transparent to sound waves. This is 
considered as an important quality within an EKO-Canopy where many needs have to be satisfied. 
ETFE is low flammable rated as B-s1 d0. No drops occur and the material itself has a very low 
calorific value. Although the cushions are self-venting at >200 °C as the cushions detaches from the 
frames, additional smoke ventilation is required in case of fire, as the material become toxic when 
burned. Although the ETFE helps to stabilise the structure, it can in general stand without ETFE 
which is important in regards to fire safety. 
==PAGE:39==
2.4 - Preliminary investigations 
Page 37 
 
ETFE itself is a clean material without use of plasticisers and is 100 % recyclable. Vector Foiltec´s 
policy is that they return in all the products. The Texlon system has been awarded an Environmental 
Product Declaration (EPD).  
ETFE can resist high pressure. This include several meters of snow. But the pressure within the 
cushions can only resist less pressure. Therefore A slope of 8-10° on the ETFE roof should be 
enough to avoid water collection from snow melt to occur. With smaller slopes wire installation 
should be installed within the ETFE cushions. (Geoffrey, Vector Foiltec) 
2.4.4.1 
Optical performance of ETFE  
Light transmittance has shown to be even higher than for glass, which also appears in the graphs at 
Figure 3-10 in (2.3.2 - Light transmittance in materials). Clear ETFE also allow significantly higher 
transmittance outside the visible spectrum, including both UV-light and infrared light. Various 
vegetation requires UV-light thrive, which make ETFE ideal for covering stadium that need grass to 
grow. However, the higher transmittance will increase solar gains which can lead to overheating. 
Furthermore, the transparency of long wave radiation will allow more heat to radiate to the sky 
during the cold season and by that reduce the thermal performance.  
Varieties of films are available that allow various wavelengths to be transmitted including the solar 
control mechanism shown in (2.1.4 - ETFE constructions) Datasheets from Vector Foiltec show 
further light transmittance properties for the Texlon system, where framing is included for various 
configurations, see Appendix Part III B3  .  
Normally, ETFE do not achieve the same optical performance as glass but recent development in 
production has led to optically clear foils. An example can be seen at the photo at Figure 3-18. 
Various projects have dotted ETFE cushions with solar control. This seems to result in unclear 
cushions that can look dirty from inside and opaque from outside. 
In accordance to (2.3.2.1 - IOR (Index Of Refraction)), the influence of IOR has been considered 
for ETFE cushions. 
 
Figure 3-17 Solar incidence angle variation on an ETFE 
cushion, whereis the angle of incidence. “d” is the 
displacement of light rays. The thickness of the ETFE 
cushion is enlarged drastically in the illustration. (See 
section 2.3.2.1- IOR (Index Of Refraction) 
The curved surface of an ETFE cushion 
results in varying angles of incidence as 
illustrated on Figure 3-17. The refraction 
results in displacement of the transmitted 
light, which could lead to disturbed view 
through the ETFE cushion.  
However, the relatively small thickness 
of the ETFE foil of only 0.1 - 0.3 mm 
results in significantly smaller light 
displacement than what is seen for 
curved glass with significantly larger 
thickness, usually about 6 mm per layer.  
A visual assessment has been performed 
on a piece of ETFE indicating minor 
disturbance of the view at various angles. 
==PAGE:40==
2.4 - Preliminary investigations 
Page 38 
 
 
Figure 3-18 DWI Aachen with NOWOFLON 6235 Z-IR-cut solar control ETFE, (Source: NOWOFLON) 
 
 
Figure 3-19 Waitomo Glowworm Caves (2012), visitor centre, New Zealand A timber/wire construction with 
ETFE cushions attached on thin steel frames with integrated gutters. An elegant arching toroid, formed by 
prefabricated I-beams made of LVL (local) pine wood, interwoven and hold together by clamps, resulting in 
long uninterrupted ETFE cushions with a width of 4-5m. (Source: Wellington company & Vector Foiltec)

2.4.4.2 
Integration of PV´s 
The EKO-Canopy  seeks to be an icon for alternative energy renovation. The advantage of 
integrating PV´s (photovoltaic) into architecture is savings in electricity distribution. However, 
integration of PV´s in architecture has generally shown to be significantly more expensive in both 
production and maintenance than what is possible with large scale PV-parks.  
 
Figure 3-20 Integrated PV in ETFE cushions, AWM Carport 
Munich, Ackermann & Partner (Source: Makmax.com) 
PV´s has been integrated into a 9600 m2 
large 3-layer ETFE roof covering for a 
carport in Munich, see Figure 3-20. The 
production from the PV´s is 130 
MWh/year corresponding to 590 kWh 
per cushion. The upper layer is separately 
fixed to allow access to the PV´s.  
New technologies of printable PV´s are 
quickly becoming more efficient. Plenty 
of research is currently being done in the 
field with promising results. (Leach 
2014) (Nichols & Mackenzie 2011) 
Nowofol is investigating print with PV´s 
on ETFE (Morris 2012). 
 
Figure 3-21 Transparent flexible PV´s (Source: Alsvid.com) 
A contact to the Swedish Alsvid Solar 
has been established during the thesis. 
Alsvid Solar researches in transparent 
organic PV´s and the contact has 
founded a contact between Alsvid and 
Vector Foiltec as a result of the thesis.  
Overheating of the air in the EFTE 
cushion could reduce the electricity 
production of conventional PV´s. 
However, Alsvid Solar PV´s become 
more efficient at higher temperatures of 
up to around 100 °C.  
Figure 3-22 Illustration showing idea of PV integration within 
ETFE cushions combined with solar control (Source: 
(ReRDM Project 13 2013) link, post-processed figure) 
Figure 3-22 shows an ideas of combining 
PV´s with the “Vario” solar control 
mechanism also used within Kingsdale 
School, see (2.1.4 - ETFE constructions). 
ETFE has higher IR and UV-light 
transmittance than glass, which should 
be considered. It allows more of the suns 
energy to reach the PV´s which can be 
used by some types of PV´s but can also 
damage other types.  
The solar control mechanism also allows 
control of the U-value as the layer in the 
middle attaches to the upper layer.   
The light transmittance is critically 
reduced when using the Texlon Vario 
system, with 0.32/0.19 LT and g-value of 
0.42/0.23 depending on the opening.

2.5 
Weather data 
The Nordic counties are characterised by seasonal weather applicable for the northern hemisphere. 
This include a hot summer season and a cold winter season with two seasonal stages in between, 
autumn and spring. The low temperatures during the colder season(s) require occupied buildings to 
be heated and higher temperatures during the summertime often require sufficient ventilation and 
solar shading in order to keep temperatures within the comfort zones. 
2.5.1 
Temperatures in Nordic counties  
Annual temperatures for selected Nordic cities are shown in Figure 3-23. The monthly averages are 
given as well as an overall annual average shown at the right side of the included table.  
 
Figure 3-23 Monthly averaged temperatures for selected Nordic cities (Data from holiday-weather.com) 
The warmest of the chosen cities is Copenhagen, Denmark, (Green graph) with an annual average 
of 8.5 °C and the coldest month being January with a monthly average of 0 °C and the warmest 
months being July and August with monthly averages of 17 °C. The coldest annual average 
temperature is found in Reykjavik, Iceland, (Dark blue) with an annual average of 4.7 °C. Reykjavik 
is further more the city with lowest monthly temperature fluctuations, ranging from 0-11 °C, 
whereas the highest monthly fluctuations are found in Helsinki, Finland, ranging from -6 to 17 °C.  
The temperatures in Stockholm (Red) are representing good average monthly temperatures for the 
represented Nordic cities. The coldest months are January and February with monthly averages of -3 
°C. The hottest months are slightly warmer than the average for the Nordic countries with July 
being the hottest with 18 °C. Temperatures in Oslo, Norway (Yellow graph), are very similar to 
temperatures in Stockholm with an annual average being 0.1 °C lower than the annual average of 
Stockholm which is found to be 6.9 °C.

2.6 - Preliminary investigations
