2.6 
Snow management 
The EKO-Canopy construction is thought as a lightweight construction with large spans of nearly 
30 m in the short direction. In a climate with relatively large amount of snowfall, snow loads can be 
seen as a critical and dimensioning load factor for a construction with these configurations. One way 
to deal with the snow is to design the canopy to be able to carry the loads of snow collecting during 
a winter. However, this is likely to require considerably amounts of support resulting in a heavier 
and more expensive construction with larger environmental footprint. Having the canopy covered 
by snow can additionally be critical for the daylight conditions and will reduce passive solar gains 
considerably during the time of year where most needed. 
In other words, it is critical that the snow is being managed in some way, when designing the 
canopy. The Bovieran project manage the snow by actively blowing hot air on the inside of the 
Bovieran glass roofs. (See section 2.1.1) The slope of the atrium roof of 10° may allow some of the 
snow to slide off. However, the main part is actively melted.  
2.6.1 
Melting of snow  
December-March are the months with most snowfall in Stockholm with a total precipitation of 138 
mm. With a water density of 1.0 kg/m3, this compares to 138 kg/m2 snow that has to be melted 
each winter.  
Snow is crystalized water. The phase change from solid ice to liquid water, require 330 kJ/kg, which 
is equal to 0.0917 kWh/kg. By multiplying this with the annual snowfall of 138 kg/m2, 12.7 
kWh/m2 is needed. 
With a canopy area of 2050 m2, a total of 26 MWh is needed to melt the fallen snow on the Canopy 
in an average winter season in Stockholm. With the total floor area of 13400 m2 of the two 
Dragonvägen buildings, this energy consumption will correspond to 1.9 kWh/m2. If the heating is 
delivered with district of 0.8 SEK/kWh this will result in an annual  
The calculation is a simplification, which does not include that some of the snow is melted by the 
sun and times with positive outdoor temperatures. On the other hand, the additional heat losses 
occurring during the time of year where temperatures are mostly freezing, are not included as well.  
2.6.2 
Snow management – passive strategies 
To avoid the high energy consumption for snow melting and to allow daylight and passive solar heat 
gains to access the canopy, a strategy is preferred that does not include high energy consumption. 
Removing the snow instead of melting it is another solution. However, this is considered to require 
too much maintenance if done manually and could also require additional safety advances.   
A steeper slope of the roof will in general allow snow too fall off at a higher rate. However, the 
surface smoothness, the type of snow and wind conditions are other parameters that influences how 
the snow behave. Risks of blocking fire escape ways are also to be considered along with risks of 
drifting snow. Additionally, unbalanced snow loads can be a critical load factor that should be 
considered. (D.A. Taylor 1983) 
Melting of the inner part of the snow cover will result in a low friction between the snow and the 
canopy roof, allowing the snow to drift more easily. The method of having hot air blown at the 
internal part of the canopy roof is considered as a good idea in several aspects and can be used as a 
backup snow management strategy. 
A slope of 8-10° on the ETFE roof should be enough to avoid water collection from snow melt to 
occur. With smaller slopes wire installation should be installed within the ETFE cushions. 
(Geoffrey, Vector Foiltec) 
==PAGE:47==
2.6 - Canopy geometry investigations 
Page 45 
 
3 
Canopy geometry investigations  
This section seeks to investigate possible geometries of the EKO-Canopy, as many important 
parameters are influenced by the geometry of the canopy. The canopy surface area for transmission 
is investigated along with wind load investigations. An overview of influencing parameters is shown 
in Figure 2-1 in (PART 2:1.1 - Integrated, iterative design). The geometries are mainly based on 
structural principles for ETFE constructions with higher degree of flexibility in form and structural 
principle.  
3.1.1 
Canopy surface area  
The surface area of the EKO-Canopy is interesting due to the area for thermal heat transfer. 
Another interesting aspect is the material usage. Although material usage can be difficult to 
compare, as the structural principle is a dictating parameter, surface area investigations can still be 
used to make a basis to decide in which direction to go with the design.  
The canopy is thought as an alternative to conventional energy renovation. Besides improvement of 
technical installations to reduce energy consumption, a thorough renovation typically includes 
additional insulation in walls and replacement of windows with better insulating performance to 
reduce heat losses of the existing building envelope. This approach reduces the U-value resulting in 
lower heat losses, see eq (3-2). 
The total heat loss from a building can basically be defined by the U-value multiplied by the 
envelope area and the temperature difference between indoor and outdoor, as shown in eq (3-2) 
Q = U*A*T 
 
 U is the U-value [W/m2K] 
 A is the area of transmission [m2]  
T is the temperature difference between inside and outside 
(Tindoor – Toutdoor) measured in Kelvin or °C 
Q is the total heat loss or heat flow [W] 
 
eq (3-2) 
The formula in eq (3-2) show that alternative ways of reducing the heat losses can be done by 
reducing either the area of transmission A, or to reduce the temperature difference ΔT. Instead of 
adding the thermal improvements directly at the facades, the EKO-Canopy create a new envelope 
that connect the existing facades with a new thermal zone. The new thermal zone is expected to 
have higher temperatures than the current outside temperatures resulting in a reduction in heat 
losses. 
Another way to look at how the EKO-Canopy influences the heat flow in eq (3-2) is the area of 
transmission. Where the existing envelope is defined by the building facades, the new construction 
extends the building envelope to the canopy envelope with a completely new geometry. The new 
envelope defines a new area of transmission. As a way to assess initial considerations about the 
geometry, various canopy forms are compared to the area of the existing facades, shown Figure 
3-26.

3.1.2 
Minimal surface 
As described, a reduction of the canopy envelope area will reduce area for thermal transmission and 
could potentially reduce the amount of materials, resulting in lower costs and lower environmental 
footprint. However, the latter depends on the construction design, as more complex structures are 
likely to increase material usage and costs per square meter. The ETFE material allows for flexible 
forms and construction methods. Examples of frames constructed with wires have already been 
built and could be a possible solution for the EKO-Canopy.  
 
Figure 3-30 Minimal surface created by a bubble 
(Source: soapbubble.dk) 
A minimal surface is characterized as a 
surface with the smallest possible surface area 
for given boundary conditions. Minimal 
surfaces are found in nature where the surface 
tension of liquids can generate approximated 
minimal surfaces.  
Minimal surfaces can also be expressed with 
mathematics, where a simple mathematical 
formula can generate a minimal surface with 
boundaries close to matching those for the 
EKO-Canopy, see eq (3-3). 
 
 
log(cos(x)/cos(y)) 
 
x = [-Pi/2 ..Pi/2], y = [-Pi/2 ..Pi/2] 
eq (3-3) 
 
 
Figure 3-31 Schurks surface plotted in Maple 15, the 
plot data is shown in appendixxxx 
When eq (3-3) is plotted in 3d coordinates, 
a minimal surface named “Schurks Surface” 
becomes visible. 
As seen, the surface boundaries at the top 
does not follow the boundaries directly, as 
the surface curves inwards, where the 
buildings are constructed with straight lines. 
It can be proofed that the surface forms the 
smallest possible surface area for the given 
boundaries. 
The surface area of the mathematical 
equation can be found with a Jacobian 
Vector Calculus for defined boundaries. 
However, these investigations have not 
been taking further, as another method has 
been used with higher flexibility in terms of 
boundary conditions.  
SketchUp is used to generate minimal surfaces using a plug-in from tensile-structures.de. The plugin 
allows generation of minimal surfaces from selected boundary edges. The plugin calculates an 
approximated minimal surface existing of triangular surfaces. The plug-in also allows pneumatically 
strained processing of generated minimal surfaces.

3.2 
Canopy wind load investigations 
Wind load investigations are performed to investigate how various canopy geometries are affected 
by wind and how they compare. Wind can influence the canopy design in several ways:  
• 
The relatively large closed surfaces of a canopy construction make wind forces a dimensioning 
factor for the construction. Designing the canopy to be as little influenced by wind loads as 
possible can result in a lighter and less expensive construction. Minimizing frame sizes are 
important for the daylight and how the canopy is experienced, this is further addressed in (4.4.10 
- Canopy framing geometry)  
• 
Limiting air pressure of the canopy envelope will reduce infiltration of outside air. Infiltration is 
considered to be a significant factor for maintenance of the climate inside the canopy, which is 
important for both thermal comfort as well as energy savings. (See chapter 5 for more info) 
• 
Wind pressure assessment can help to locate efficient natural ventilation openings in the canopy, 
to prevent overheating in the warm season. 
• 
Wind influences snow drifts and destributes on roofs which can help a passive snow 
management strategy (Hjorth-Hansen et al. 2000) 
• 
Adding additional building mass to the relatively large building blocks is likely to result in higher 
wind speeds around the buildings, which can reduce the comfort level for pedestrians.  
The investigations are mainly focused on the wind close to the buildings and the pressure on the 
buildings. AFD is used to investigate how wind affects various canopy geometries. This include 
pressure measured on the construction surfaces and wind velocities around the building.  
3.2.1 
Wind analysis with Flow Design 
(Theory about the AFD and the reliability has been discussed with Henrik Brun Hansen, Fluid Mechanics 
Specialist at GEA Process Engineering A/S, see Q/A in appendixxx) Further information was found in (Nielsen 
2007) as well as on (autodesk.com)  
Investigations are performed with AFD (Autodesk Flow Design). The CFD software uses 
Reynolds-Averaged Navier-Stokes (RANS) equations to calculate steady state wind flows. The used 
turbulence model is not known but k-epsilon or k-omega is likely. Meshing is automatically 
generated by Flow Design, whereas advanced meshing is not possible. Thorough CFD analysis or 
wind tunnel simulations are required to get results that can be considered as representative for the 
real conditions. However, the wind flow simulations can give a good indication of how various 
designs are influenced by wind.  
As the simulations are performed to investigate different parameters, various design wind speeds are 
considered. As wind is not compressed considerably under normal occurring wind conditions, the 
flows and velocities can roughly be seen as independent of scale. This means that an increased wind 
velocity of e.g. 38 % simulated with one design wind speed will result in the same % increase for 
other design wind velocities. A test has been performed, both showing 38 % increase, see 
appendixxx. 
However, pressure on the building structure behave differently with an exponential correlation. 
Therefore the chosen wind speed for the simulations is chosen as the design wind speed for 
structural analysis. The recommended design wind speed of 24 m/s according to (DS/EN 1991-1-4 
2010) is chosen. Other considerations are addressed in appendixxx.  
Simulation output 
Units 
Other expression 
Output shown 
Pressure 
pascal [Pa] 
[N/m2] or [kg/s2m] 
Colouring of surfaces 
Wind velocity 
[m/s] 
3.6 [km/h]

3.2 - Canopy geometry investigations 
Page 53 
 
3.2.2 
Simulation setup 
Geometries are modelled in SketchUp and exported to OBJ. file format and imported directly to 
AFD.  
Setup of simulation domain boundaries are shown in Figure 3-39. Boundary dimensions are setup 
with respect to “Workbench tutorial 05 wind around buildings” (Fan 2015). However the size of the 
domain boundaries are reduced to reduce simulation time. Furthermore, larger boundaries are 
assumed to result in a less detailed mesh generation. The main focus of the simulations is to get an 
indication about how different designs perform. The model is simplified without including other 
surrounding buildings than the two adjacent. Therefore, the accuracy of larger boundaries are not 
considered to give more reliable results.  
 
Figure 3-39 CFD Setup of tunnel boundaries. Blue box defines tunnel boundaries for which the wind flows 
are simulated. Boundary measures compared to the building are shown. Red box defines where flow lines are 
visible. 
After the setup of domain boundaries and design wind speeds, the simulation is started. At the start 
of the simulation AFD show the simulation as “Transient” (see upper right corner in Figure 3-39). It 
takes about a half an hour for the performed simulations to be shown as “Stabilized”.  
Figure 3-40 Canopy double symmetry 
A simulation drag plot graph is available, see 
black graph at Figure 3-41. The drag plot is 
compared with the averaged drag plot 
coefficient of the simulation This can be used 
to assess a possible steady state condition. 
The simulation at Figure 3-41 was simulated 
for about 2 hours without achieving steady 
state. However the condition is assessed 
“Stabilized” and the maximum and minimum 
velocities and pressures were stable since 
AFD showed the simulation as “Stabilized”. 
As shown at Figure 3-40 the canopy can be 
simplified as being double symmetrical. This 
means that wind simulations performed from 
one side can make up for both sides.

3.2.3 
Simulations results 
Wind roses for Stockholm are shown in Appendix Part III C.1b for comparison.  
 
Figure 3-41 Stabilized simulation of rectangular canopy simulated for about 2 hours. The simulation showed 
status as “Stabilized” after 30 minutes. The drag coefficient and “Average drag coefficient“ did not find steady 
state condition. Results of wind velocity and pressure did not change considerably after the simulation reached 
“Stabilized” status. Simulation performed with wind from west/east with a wind speed of 24 m/s. 
A result image for the rectangular shaped canopy simulated with wind from west/east is shown at 
Figure 3-41. The figure shows the canopy wall to be exposed to a high pressure (red surface) of 
more than 300 Pa (or 300 N/m2) on the major part of the large surface area. A suction (dark blue 
parts) of about 400 Pa is seen at the upper edge of the canopy. The rest of the canopy roof is 
exposed to a suction of about 0-200 Pa (green to turquoise).  
The highest wind speeds are found at the space between the buildings to be around 31 m/s which is 
30 % higher than the design wind speed of 24 m/s. Turbulence is seen behind the buildings. The 
back side of the canopy shows to have low pressure, which can be seen in Appendix Part III C.1c 
where a simulation with a 45° rotation is also shown.  
 
Figure 3-42 Minimal canopy surface: Wind: east/west. Design wind speed: 24 m/s.  
Figure 3-42 show simulated wind for the minimal surface. Wind speeds are found to be highest at 
the top of the canopy surface, where orange flow lines occurs that matches approximately 32 m/s. 
==PAGE:57==
3.2 - Canopy geometry investigations 
Page 55 
 
This correspond in 33 % increase compared to the design wind speed of 24 m/s. The accelerated 
wind speeds at the top of the canopy is seen as beneficial for removing possible snow collection at 
the flat part of the minimal canopy surface. Wind from west is the dominating wind direction in 
winter, see wind rose in Appendix Part III C.1b. However, wind coming from other directions does 
not seem to have the same effect on the geometry. This can be seen in Appendix Part III C.1c  
where simulations with rotation of 45° and 90° are included.  
The accelerated wind speeds at the top of the canopy result in negative pressure that result in an 
updraft of the canopy surface, similar to the principle of an airplane wing. The suction is found to 
be up to about 450 Pa at the blue area on top of the canopy. Although the area is of limited size 
there are still a relatively high suction of about 200-300 Pa present at the whole upper part of the 
minimal surface. A lightweight ETFE roof weighs about 8 kg/m2. A suction of 450 Pa corresponds 
to a counterweight of nearly 50 kg/m2, meaning that the canopy will have to be well fastened.  
Seen from another perspective, the pressure distribution indicates efficient natural ventilation 
conditions for a summer situation where wind speeds are dominating from east/west. See wind rose 
in Appendix Part III C.1b. With ventilation openings at the lower part of the canopy and ventilation 
openings at the roof area, an efficient natural ventilation should be available. Suction at the minimal 
surface upper part is also found for north/south wind directions (90° rotation). But for a 45° 
rotation turbulence seems to be resulting in doubtful ventilation efficiency. Natural ventilation of 
the canopy is further addressed in (5.4.2.3 - Natural ventilation in hot season). 
Wind analysis of the minimal surface with included triangular glass walls (described in Figure 3-38 
page 3-51) is shown in Figure 3-43. The shape and the results are similar to the previously described 
minimal surface. Less overall suction at the roof seems to be occurring, with the peak suction (blue 
area) at the edge between the triangular walls and the minimal surface. However, the critically high 
increase of suction for the specific area can be influenced by poor mesh generation at this area.  
 
Figure 3-43 Minimal surface, triangular walls, (described in Figure 3-38 page 3-51). Wind: east/west, 24 m/s. 
The triangular walls seem to be increasingly exposed to high pressure (red surface). The high 
pressure at the triangular walls combined with possible pulling forces from the minimal surface 
makes the structural design of the triangular walls challenging.  
Wind analysis of the geometry with added building mass is shown Figure 3-44. The geometry react 
in similar ways as what is seen for the other minimal surfaces. Although marginally higher wind 
velocities are generated above the canopy as well as slightly increased suction on the top of the 
canopy. The simulation show a (blue) spot with very high suction of more than 600 Pa . This seem 
to be an unreliable deviation which could be caused by a poor mesh generation around the point.  
 
==PAGE:58==
3.2 - Canopy geometry investigations 
Page 56 
 
 
Figure 3-44 Minimal surface with added building mass. Wind from east/west, design wind speed: 24 m/s. 
High suction forces seems to be dominating the top of the minimal surface geometries. This should 
be considered regarding structural principles. However, the accelerated wind velocities at the most 
horizontal areas of the minimal surfaces can be beneficial for removing possible snow collection. 
There is a risk, that the canopy has to be assessed as a “highly dynamic structure” if built with 
extended use of wire cables (CFD in Wind Engineering(1.2)) This will complicate the design 
extensively. 
The wind analysis can be used to assess positive and negative influence of wind on the investigated 
canopy geometries. However, the wind analysis can only be seen as rough indicators of how the 
different geometries perform when exposed to wind. Although the Dragonvägen buildings are the 
highest in the urban area, several other buildings are located around the buildings. The effect of 
these buildings are assumed to have the largest influence on the lower part of the buildings, which 
can mostly be seen as a positive difference, as wind loads on the constructions seem to be highest 
around the ground. To this come also that AFD simulate with uniform wind speeds, where wind 
speeds close to the ground are significantly lower than wind speeds higher above ground. 
The geometries are simplified and details are not included. Canopy construction frames, balconies 
on the opposing buildings as well as trees and additional surrounding buildings will all influence the 
real wind conditions.  
Although no overall conclusion can be made on basis of the wind analysis, the studies has shown to 
create a good basis for assessing various design solutions. Further analysis and assessment of other 
possible geometries can lead to a more optimized design. Additional surrounding geometries should 
be considered included along with further detailed analysis. 
 
 
==PAGE:59==
4.1 - Daylight 
Page 57 
 
4 
Daylight 
A project like the EKO-Canopy is considered to be difficult to implement without well documented 
strategies that can secure good daylight conditions for residents. The main focus of the daylight 
investigations is to give an indication about how an EKO-Canopy influences daylight within existing 
apartments. The quality of daylight within the canopy is also discussed but will not be the main 
focus for the studies. Furthermore the investigations also attempt to establish a space of solutions 
that can help in the initial design phase by identifying important parameters. Analyses of canopy 
daylight transmission are investigated for critically located apartments along with investigations of 
reflectance of canopy ground and façade reflectance of existing buildings. Simulations performed for 
a case with thicker walls are included for comparison with a conventional renovation with additional 
insulation and analysis of simple construction frame geometries makes basis for discussion. 
The studies are based on simulations with approximated values that matches the actual conditions as 
well as possible. Simulations are based on available material including CAD drawings and photos. 
However, uncertainties are present for both the simulation setup and for the simulation tool. The 
investigations give an indication of the actual conditions and can be used to compare parameter 
variations of different solutions.  
4.1 
Daylight introduction 
It is well documented that sufficient daylight is important for the wellbeing of humans and are 
influencing us in many ways (Figueiro et al. 2002). We are designed to spend the most of our lives 
outdoors (Christoffersen 2006), but surveys show that Europeans on average spend about 90 % of 
their lives indoors (European Comission 2003). This percentage could be even more for people 
living in the Nordic countries. 
Daylight has even proved to affect the health of humans as it helps to control hormone production 
within the human body (Auer 2015) (Rogers et al. 2015) Page 23-24. The circadian rhythm is mostly 
affected by light in the blue spectrum whereas the limbic system in the brain mainly reacts on the 
red light spectrum. (Rogers et al. 2015) (See 2.3.2 - Light transmittance in materials) 
Lack of daylight can lead to uncontrolled production of melatonin which can lead to SAD (Seasonal 
Affective Disorder). This is especially a well-known problem in the Nordic countries during winter 
time where daylight is limited, where SAD impacts about 10-20 % population (Additude 2016). A 
sun path diagram for Stockholm show that the sun is only up for 6 hours during the darkest period 
the 21nd of December with a sun angle of only 9°. (Appendix Part III D.1) 
The experience of daylight plays an essential factor for how we perceive a space and its architectural 
quality. View to the outside is additionally an essential feature of windows. The variations in daylight 
and the seasonal changes are important for the wellbeing of humans. This is mentioned in 
regulations and standards all over the Nordic countries. (Johnsen & Christoffersen 2008) (Rogers et 
al. 2015) (GBCD 2014) 
When no daylight is available, artificial lighting is used during waking hours. Artificial lighting 
constitutes a large part of the energy consumption in buildings. With sufficient daylight the need for 
artificial lighting is reduced. Although it is difficult to predict how people in residential buildings 
tend to turn on and off artificial lighting, there is higher tendency that light is turned off in daytime. 
4.2 
Daylight assessment  
There are many factors that influence the quality of daylight in buildings and many ways to assess 
them. As described in 4.1, sufficient daylight is important for humans, whereas the most common 
approach of assessing daylight within buildings is evaluation of the amount of daylight coming into 
buildings, where DF (daylight factor) is commonly used. Daylight investigations in this report are 
performed using simulations of DF, which is described in (4.2.1) below.  
==PAGE:60==
4.2 - Daylight 
Page 58 
 
Other possible daylight assessment methods are described in the following two sections to give a 
better understanding of other daylight parameters that could potentially influence the daylight design 
parameters for the EKO-Canopy. 
4.2.1 
Daylight Factor (DF) 
Daylight Factor (DF) is widely used to assess daylight within buildings and is considered to be a 
good indicator for assessing daylight conditions for the dealing residential buildings. DF is based on 
a steady state condition with a predefined uniform sky condition. An advantage of this is that the 
DF simulations are valid for all locations and orientations, also making them applicable to other 
Nordic countries. 
 
Figure 3-45 Illustration showing example 
of 2.0 %DF (Source: (NLK 2014) 
The DF is defined by the percentage of daylight 
illumination on an internal plane in a building 
compared to the outdoor illumination under CIE 
overcast sky, see an example with 2.0 %DF at the 
illustration in Figure 3-45. Simulations are 
performed in steady state conditions with diffuse sky 
conditions illuminating 10000 lux. The Daylight 
Factor expression is given as: 
DF = 
!!!"#$%"&'
!!"#$!!%
!∙100% 
 
 
E is the illuminance in [lux] 
 
eq (3-4) 
 
In general, Einternal is measured on a horizontal surface at table height between 0.80-0.85 m above 
floor height. This is further explained in (4.2.2 - Daylight assessment in Nordic countries) 
The DF changes considerably depending on the angling and distance to the daylight source which is 
typically a window. The DF closest to a window can easily exceed 20% DF and at the same time be 
lower than 1% just few meters into the room. The distribution of daylight is important in order to 
achieve useful daylight. The Swedish standard SS 914201 takes this issue into account and will be 
used in this report to evaluate daylight within the investigated apartments. See (4.2.2) 
People are in general more flexible when it comes to daylight within residential buildings. There are 
better options for controlling daylight individually and more flexibility to move around according to 
present daylight conditions. Furthermore there are less people to satisfy per room compared to open 
office spaces and schools.  
DF can give good indications about the amount of available daylight within a room. Daylight 
evaluation with DF is especially useful for the given case, where higher DF are considered to result 
in better daylight conditions, as relatively low daylight levels are available. Since we are dealing with a 
residential building, glare is not considered to be an issue in the same way as it would be for schools 
and offices.  
Daylight investigations in this report are simulated with VDV simulation software.  
==PAGE:61==
4.2 - Daylight 
Page 59 
 
4.2.2 
Daylight assessment in Nordic countries 
Daylight is evaluated with varying methods within the Nordic countries. No strict defined rules are 
applicable, however standards require in general that daylight within buildings should be sufficient 
and that view to surroundings are important. Various instructions and guidance as well as 
certifications are available. 
The Swedish Standard SS 914201 is chosen for the main assessment method for the daylight studies 
as the case study is located in Stockholm, Sweden. According to SS 914201, DF has to be measured 
0.80 m above floor at a point 1 meter from the darkest sidewall at half of the room depth: 
“1 m from darkest sidewall at half room depth 0.8m above floor”  (Translated from (SIS 1989)) 
“The calculation must take surrounding buildings and external shading structures, fixed screens, etc. into 
account” (Miljöbyggnad 2014) 
In case the daylight studies are evaluated for other Nordic countries than Sweden, other standards, 
guidance and selected certification systems are included in Table 3-3.

“Surroundings” would often refer to “outside”, however atriums and canopies can also be 
considered as “surroundings” (Source!??). This is an important discussion for the EKO-Canopy 
project. Sufficient daylight and vegetation can be considered to be important qualities for 
“surroundings”.  
4.2.3 
Daylight assessment considerations 
DF is defined for overcast diffuse sky conditions and can be seen as daylight conditions on a day 
with critical daylight. Under these conditions, the daylight is delivered equally from the whole sky, 
meaning the illuminance on each window orientations are the same.  
In Stockholm and Nordic countries, the sun is moving in a path during a day, starting in east to 
south and finish west. (see Appendix IIIA.1), meaning there will be more illumination on south 
oriented windows than on north oriented windows at times where the sun light is not spread equally 
by an overcast sky. On a clear sunny day, the outside illumination can reach more than 100000 lux. 
Under these conditions, the main part of the sun light will be illuminated from the sun´s position on 
the sky.  
Dynamic daylight simulations that include hourly daylight assessment during a whole year is 
possible, where also specific location and orientation are given. Daylight Autonomy (DA) is 
commonly used for this dynamic daylight simulation, where the percentage of annual daytime or 
working hours that exceeds 300 lux at specific points, are simulated. Adjustable solar shading and 
other controls can also be simulated with such dynamic simulation tools.  
Too much direct sunlight can result in glare issues, which is mainly considered a problem in offices 
and schools, where working places should achieve the best possible lighting comfort for working 
tasks. It is more simple to define what good daylight on a working desk is, where specific tasks has 
to be performed and usually within the specific working hours. Therefore Useful Daylight 
Illumination (UDI) is defined as illuminance between 100-2000 lux. Using this, it is valuable to 
assess the daylight conditions during a year and investigate the percentage of hours where useful 
daylight is available.  
Overheating issues are typically more critical in offices and schools and other buildings where more 
people are present per square meter than what is typically seen in apartments. To this also comes 
additional heat production from computers and other equipment. Critical periods with high solar 
radiation are influencing the sharply defined upper threshold of 2000 lux. This limit for which 
daylight is considered to be “not useful” is being discussed, as illuminance levels high above 2000 
lux can be perceived as comfortable as long as contrast ratios are not too high and overheating is 
not a problem. (Advanced Buildings 2013)  
Nevertheless, atriums can have a positive influence on UDI for rooms facing the atrium, as high 
illuminances above 2000 lux are avoided (Auer 2015) page 52-56 and (Landgren & Erikshøj 2015). 
This also indicate less glare issues, however glare is typically not seen as a problem in residential 
buildings, where people are more flexible to move around. Additionally, people have deviating 
comfort requirements within residential buildings making it difficult to decide what good daylight 
conditions are. 
The report “En genomgång av svenska dagsljuskrav” from May 2015 states that there should be taken 
further analysis methods in use in Sweden, for assessing daylight conditions in buildings (Rogers et 
al. 2015). Proposals include the above discussed methods and considerations. As mentioned 
residential buildings are more complex to evaluate for these further daylight parameters, whereas the 
DF method is still considered to be a good assessment method for the concerning project.  
==PAGE:63==
4.3 - Daylight 
Page 61 
 
4.3 
Setup of VDV simulation model 
Daylight simulations are performed with the free software program Velux Daylight Visualizer 2.8.3. 
The software has passed the CIE 171:2006 test with an average error lower than 1.29 % (Labayrade 
et al. 2006)  
4.3.1 
Geometry of simulation model 
A reference simulation model is defined (REF). The simulation model is based on the geometry of 
Dragonvägen (See 0) and include a canopy with a simple design. The REF simulation model is set 
up with basis parameters matching a possible canopy design. Frames are not included for the REF 
simulation as the construction is not yet known. Simulations with frames included in the geometry 
show to have nearly identical distribution of DF, (for more information, see 4.4.10 - Canopy 
framing geometry)  
The REF simulation is used for comparison with various parameter investigations.  
The REF simulation model includes two identical building blocks including shading surfaces from 
neighbouring building blocks, see Figure 3-46. Apartments with most critical daylight conditions are 
chosen for the REF model. The location of these are assumed to be in the middle of the building at 
1st floor, 3200 mm above ground, as no apartments are located at the ground floor. A full year 
simulation of solar energy gains on the building can be seen in Appendix Part III C.1 
 
Figure 3-46 SketchUp geometry model for reference daylight simulation. Shading surfaces of neighbouring 
buildings are included. Transparency in the figure allow to see internal geometry of the apartments in focus for 
daylight assessment. North is indicated with arrow. The geometry is imported directly to VDV. 
The simulation is setup with two building blocks including two sets of north oriented rooms and 
two sets of south oriented rooms. One of each sets are facing the canopy and one of each sets are 
facing a courtyard without canopy covering. The purpose of the rooms, which does not face the 
canopy is to include simulation results for comparison, as several investigations include variations 
that both affect DF% within the canopy as well as outside the canopy.   
The distance between the two building facades is 30.0 m excluding the balcony depth of 1.75 m. 
Dragonvägen has a sloping ground from one end of the building to the other, see elevation drawing 
(Appendix IB.1). An approximated simplification of the building height of 22.0 m is used for the 
daylight investigations. This is both done to simplify the model geometry, and to achieve a 
simulation model that is easier to refer to for more common building types where the ground is not 
changing height. The simplification is not considered to influence the daylight noticeably, as the 
lowest positioned apartments are located at 1st floor. 
==PAGE:64==
4.3 - Daylight 
Page 62 
 
Parameter variations of the reference simulations are performed with the reference inputs as basis. It 
has to be taken into account that the influence of the parameter variations is likely to deviate from 
the results, if other parameters are deviating significantly from the reference setup. As basis the 
reference parameters are set rather high for the internal reflectance in the apartments corresponding 
to empty rooms.

obstruction, see Figure 3-47. The penthouse does obstruct the horizon angles for the south oriented 
windows with a few angles as seen on Figure 3-48.

4.3.3 
VDV (Velux Daylight Visualizer) REF setup 
VDV does not support specific information about how the daylight is calculated. For example 
information about number of bounces (reflections) that are calculated for each simulation are not 
directly adjustable. Instead options of adjusting resolution and render quality are available.  
Several series of simulations have been performed with various setups of the REF simulation. 
Variations in both resolution and render quality has been performed. Each simulation setup has 
been simulated at least three times to compare the stability of the simulations. Each series of setup 
was compared with each other in order to find the simulation setup giving the most reliable output 
results. Results showed smallest deviations for a simulation setup with an extra high customized 
resolution of 2400 x 1800 and render quality set to the default value for “High”. Higher render 
quality did not seem to improve the consistency of the simulations. (See Appendix Part III D.6.a, 
p39) 
VDV user manual has been used to for better understanding of the simulation setup (Visualizer 
2016) 
 
==PAGE:70==
4.3 - Daylight 
Page 68 
 
 
 
Figure 3-54 Screen shot of VDV with REF model geometry imported from SketchUp. A plan drawing is 
shown at the left, with a section through the apartment buildings and the canopy in the middle. The elevation 
section and the perspective at the right also show the facades of the neighbouring building blocks.  
Values for reflectance and transmittance are set up according to section 4.3.2 Reflectance and 
transmittance.  
As no thickness are given of the included transparent surfaces, the option of adjusting IOR values 
can be considered to be irrelevant. In order to find the influence of the IOR function, some tests 
have been performed. Results indicate that the IOR value control reduction of light transmittance 
for light transmitted through angled surfaces. (See Appendix Part III D.7, p41) 
VDV default IOR value of 1.50 is used for all transparent surfaces in all simulations, which is the 
IOR value for glass.  
4.3.4 
VDV grid points  
VDV has an option to get results of specified grid points by importing a CSV grid point file. The file 
is set up as shown in Appendix Part III D.5 
Grid points are named with abbreviations as shown at Figure 3-55 and table Table 3-7. The names 
are used in the processing of results. The first character in the abbreviation describes façade 
orientation (north or south [N/S]), next character indicates if the room is facing the canopy or away 
from the canopy [C/R], where the “R” is an abbreviation for “Reference room”. This reference 
should not be mistaken for the REF simulation. The last character indicates the room or apartment 
number from left to right [1-4].  
Example; “NC1” = [North oriented], [facing Canopy], [room 1] 
==PAGE:71==
4.3 - Daylight 
Page 69 
 
 
Figure 3-55 Grid points position on plan drawing with X-Y axis. Grid points are marked with “x” and related 
short grid point name, as described above. Grid points in rooms are positioned according to SS 914201 and 
points in canopy are located at selected places, with C6 being the center of the canopy. Points within the 
canopy are positioned with a height at the 0.8 m at the unshown Z-axis with C9, C10 and C11 located below 
the balcony overhangs. Grid points in apartments with a Z-axis value of 4.0 m, all corresponding to 0.8 m 
above floor/ground level. The exact grid point locations are given at output tables as seen in Table 3-7.  
Although VDV has been validated against CIE 171:2006 to be an accurate daylight simulation 
program (Visualizer 2016), it does still indicate an extend of inaccuracy, also described in (4.3.3). 
With goals of further improvement of the accuracy of the grid point results, test simulations have 
been performed, using an alternative grid point file with each point split into two values displaced 
+/- 1 mm at the Y-axis. (See Appendix Part III D.6a, for processed results table) 
About 2/3 of the result outputs showed same values for the adjacent points (2 mm away), and 
smaller deviations ware also found in the remaining 1/3 of the simulations – with value differences 
corresponding the window distance. The large amount of similar values indicate that the calculations 
does not vary in the same way as they do from one simulation to another, whereas the double grid 
method did not seem to improve the accuracy of simulated grid results. A possible explanation can 
be that the precise grid point results from VDV are interpolated between calculated points. 
However this has not been investigated further and the initial single-grid point method is used.

4.4.1 
Introduction to DF simulation results 
Simulation results are presented with VDV ISO contour images and grid point file results are 
processed in Excel and presented with graphs and tables. Result output is discussed continuously 
and an overall discussion of the daylight investigations can be found in (4.6 - Discussion of daylight 
investigations). Additional result output can be found in Appendix Part III D.9 
      
 
Figure 3-56 ISO contour image example of 
room NC4 showing DF% contours 
ISO contour images show the rendered DF% 
with contour lines expressed with colours as 
displayed at the figure on the left. Numbers are 
given in %DF, whereas the blue contour line 
indicates the transition where the DF is 1.0 %, 
the dark green line indicates 2.0% and so on.  
The contour lines can be used for visual 
assessment of the light distribution, as more 
details are present. The ISO contour images can 
additionally be used for assessment of DF with 
other certification systems than SS 914201, 
including the DGNB certification system. 
 
It has to be noted that interpolation between contour lines, has to be done with awareness of the 
exponential tendency that operates, as lines are becoming increasingly closer to each other when 
closer to the window. If, for example the transition for 1.5 %DF is examined, the contour line will 
be closer to the dark green line indicating 2.0 %DF than to the blue contour line for the case above. 
In order to achieve the lowest credited DF within DGNB-DK certification system 1.5 %DF in half 
of the room is needed.  
The two points marked according to SS, are both indicated with 1.1%DF which are the values for 
the specific point locations. For the most of the results, these points are not marked at the ISO 
contour images, but will be presented in tables instead. 
All grid point simulation results are processed in Excel. The results are divided by colour as follows; 
Values measured within the canopy is indicated with a light blue colour 
Values measured within apartment facing the canopy is indicated with green colour 
Values measured within apartments without influence of a canopy covering are indicated with a yellow colour 
Additionally, DF result values are marked with colours, indicating the level of daylight compared to 
the Swedish Miljöbyggnad certification, see Table 3-6.  
Table 3-6 DF evaluation colours categorisation according to (Miljöbyggnad 2014) certification with included

and discussed along/continuously. Geometry variations are shown with illustrative SketchUp 
models, also used for import to VDV.

Results from Table 3-9 show that the DF within apartments facing the canopy achieved roughly 70 
% of the DF of the corresponding rooms not facing the canopy. The reduction in DF is in good 
consistency with the canopy light transmittance of 0.70 for the REF simulation. Results show a 
further reduction of DF for the north oriented rooms with an average of 66%. The geometry has 
been checked carefully and the shading surfaces have the same measures as the building blocks. 
However, the geometry is simplified, which could lead to smaller variations. The exclusion of 
balconies at the shading surfaces are seen as the deviation that can have the largest influence. Still 
this should influence both north and south oriented rooms, which does not have the same tendency.

4.4.2.3 
DF Results check for REF  
In Table 3-10, the manually selected DF values from the ISO contour images (4.4.2.1) are compared 
to the DF values found for the REF grid point results from Table 3-8. The grid point file values 
comply well with the ISO contour image with a total average deviation of 0 %, given at the lowest 
right corner in Table 3-10. 
The largest deviation is found at point NR2.2 with a deviation of 12 %. Although the points at the 
ISO contour images was done manually with great care, and even a ruler was used to find the point 
locations, it is seen that point NR2.2 is placed a little too far from the window in the ISO contour 
image in Figure 3-60. The manual method was found to be imprecise, therefore the grid point files 
are used.  
Table 3-10 Deviation of ISO contour DF values from REF grid point values. The values are manually inserted 
from the ISO contour images shown at Figure 3-60 and Figure 3-61. 
 
The total average value of 0 % show that even if the single grid points has uncertainties, average 
values are levelling out the differences. Average values are considered to be a good way to compare 
results between simulations for the grid point values as well.

None of the apartment rooms achieves the BR15 recommendations of minimum 2 %DF in half of

The canopy shows to have critical influence on DF assessment when categorized by DGNB-DK. 
Three out of the four north oriented rooms has high enough DF to achieve the lowest credit with 
existing conditions, but no credits are achieved for the rooms when covered by a canopy (REF). It 
has to be said that the certification system requires all rooms to achieve the required DF in order to 
get any credits, meaning the existing building would fail to get credit for DF anyway. The canopy 
easily scores maximum credits when it comes to DF%. 
4.4.2.5 
Room average DF values 
VDV include the possibility to mark up frames at the contour images, showing the average DF% 
value for the selected area. Average DF values have been selected within Figure 3-60 and Figure 
3-61. The results are shown in Table 3-12, where also the deviation from the grid point values is 
shown at the right side. The table is included to compare the grid points defined by SS 914201 with 
average DF values.  
It is seen that the room average values are significantly higher than the averages for the grid point 
values, with the north oriented rooms being about the double in average and the south oriented 
rooms being about 25-30% higher on average. The results show that the high Daylight Factors at 
the north oriented windows has a significant influence on the average DF values. The ISO contour 
lines at Figure 3-59 shows clearly the difference between north and south oriented rooms, with only 
the north oriented rooms reaching DF values high enough for the contour lines.

Table 3-16 Grid point results for simulation performed with balcony glazing. Results for REF simulation are 
included below for comparison with the south oriented rooms influenced by the canopy highlighted with red 
square. 
 
 
Although the balcony glazing has been set to 0.80 LT the average reduction in DF is simulated to be 
34 % instead of the 20 % reduction that would compare 0.80 LT with simple calculation not taking 
the angle of incidence into account. The further reduction in DF can be explained by the high 
incidence angle for which the light is transmitted through the balcony glazing. See Appendix Part III 
D.7 for more information about how VDV calculate angled light transmittance. 
4.4.5 
Minimal surface  
 
Figure 3-66 Illustration of minimal surface  
A simulation with a minimal surface is 
performed. The light transmittance is kept at 
0.70 as for the REF simulation and no frames 
are included in the geometry. More information 
about the modelling and measures of the 
minimal surface can be found in (3.1.2 - 
Minimal surface)  
 
ISO contour image output can be found in Appendix Part III .  
==PAGE:84==
4.4 - Daylight 
Page 82 
 
Table 3-17 Grid point results for simulated DF for the minimal surface.  
 
Results show DF reductions of 3 % within south oriented rooms and 2 % within north oriented 
rooms for simulations performed with the minimal construction compared to the flat canopy 
geometry used in the REF simulation. The source to the reduction is not known, however there can 
be several explanations for this; 
1. Reflected light from the upper part of the façade corners of the opposing building, has to 
travel through the minimal surface two additional times.  
2. The angle of incidence influences the light transmittance. It is difficult to tell if the minimal 
surface results in overall more angled received light. Test simulations have been made that 
show that VDV include reduction of LT for angled illumination to some extend depending 
on the IOR value. See Appendix Part III D7   
However, the variations can be considered to be of smaller importance compared to other 
parameters. Additionally, the framing system is not included for these simulations, which is likely to 
have greater influence on the DF as well as view through the construction.  
 
Figure 3-67 Dragonvägen plan drawing of large 
apartment at west gable wall. Plan cut in minimal 
canopy construction is shown with black stippled lines 
for the 7th floor. 
The minimal surface will be covering the view 
in front of the building end apartments at the 
upper floors, which could be seen as 
unacceptable, as the covering can be considered 
as a permanent glazing in front of the balconies.  
However, the most concerned apartments have 
windows at the gable walls. For gable wall 
apartments with balconies facing the canopy, 
new windows and even balconies can be 
installed to upgrade the large apartments (see 
red marking at Figure 3-67) The figure show

construction at the 7th floor.

4.4.6 
Additional insulation in walls 
 
Figure 3-68 Illustration of wall extension 
Simulations are performed with thicker external 
walls corresponding to a case with additional 
insulation. The goal is to see how the thicker 
walls influence the DF compared to the REF 
case with canopy covering. 
Wall thickness is extended 250mm outwards 
from 150 mm to 400 mm. Windows are 
likewise moved 250 mm outwards. As a result, 
balconies are reduced to 1500 mm in depth as 
seen in Figure 3-68. Window transmittance is 
kept at 0.80 LT as for the existing windows, 
although a conventional renovation could 
include 3 layer glazed windows as well as 
solutions to avoid cold bridges formed by the 
balcony sidewalls and floors. 
 
Figure 3-69 ISO contour image from simulation with 400 mm walls compared to REF simulation with wall 
thickness of 150 mm.  
==PAGE:86==
4.4 - Daylight 
Page 84 
 
Table 3-18 Grid point DF values for simulation with 400 mm walls. Note that wall thickness is increased for 
all walls, inclusive the walls facing the canopy, for that reason the REF simulation results are included below 
for comparison. Rooms not facing the canopy are highlighted (red square) for the simulation with 400 mm 
walls, therefore rooms facing the canopy are highlighted at the REF results table (Below).  
 
 
Simulation results show a reduction of 22-23 % for the north oriented rooms with thicker walls 
compared to existing conditions. The reduction for the canopy is 34 % compared to existing 
conditions. South oriented rooms seem to be significantly less influenced by the additional wall 
thickness, with a reduction of only 7-8 %, whereas the reduction due to the canopy is simulated to 
be 28%. Emphasized values for the simulation with 400 mm walls are higher than the corresponding 
emphasized rooms for the REF simulation, meaning the DF is more influenced by the canopy 
covering than the thicker walls.

primarily to get an idea about how much impact

be able to study the influence within apartments 
without canopy as well.

The simulation results shown as ISO contour images at Figure 3-77 indicate a considerable influence 
in DF for the concerned rooms with the variation of ground reflectance. However, the importance 
of the view to the outside stated in the standards has to be mentioned when the design of the 
canopy ground is evaluated. The increase in DF that can be achieved by higher reflectance of the 
canopy ground area can be considered to be of less importance compared to the potential quality of 
the space. Still, light materials within the canopy garden could result in a more inviting internal 
canopy space. 
Results of the simulation with ground reflectance of 0.10 reduce the DF about 14-17% compared to 
the REF simulation with 0.30 ground reflectance. A corresponding increase is seen for the high 
ground reflectance of 0.50, resulting in 15-16% higher DF within the critically located apartments.

Page 91 
 
The ground reflectance is considered to have considerably higher influence on the lowest positioned 
apartments, whereas smaller variations are assumed for apartments at higher levels. 
 
Figure 3-78 Influence of ground reflectance on DF% in south oriented rooms. Average values for north 
oriented rooms are included for comparison (Blue graph) 
Although the ground reflectance does have a considerable influence on the DF within the lowest 
located apartments, vegetation that can give a sense of a luxuriant outdoor space can be considered 
to be of significantly higher importance than the increase in DF for the concerned apartments. View 
to the outside space can be seen as an essential parameter in the design of an EKO-Canopy and the 
winnings of high ground reflectance can be seen as too small to make it a dictating parameter for the 
canopy landscape design. The simulations are performed with one uniform ground. In reality the 
canopy ground area would have a varying landscape. It is recommended to consider the reflectance 
of the designed space.

4.4.9 
Influence of façade reflectance 
 
Figure 3-79 Illustration facade surfaces 
Influence of façade reflectance on DF will be 
investigated for critically located apartments. 
Façade reflectance variations of +/- 0.30 
compared with the 0.60 for the REF simulation 
will be used. As seen on the figure to the left, 
the facades are simplified as being plane 
surfaces without windows and without 
balconies. This gives a clearer indication of 
what influence a façade reflectance has on the 
daylight conditions within the apartments.  
The Dragonvägen building facades are worn 
and could need replacement.  
 
Roofs are included as a part of the facades, as they are assumed to be without noteworthy influence 
on the DF within the apartments, since they are facing the sky without any possible surfaces to 
reflect the light. This is also the assumption for gable walls.  
Variations of +/- 0.30 in façade reflectance are chosen for parameter variations, resulting in 
reflectance of 0.30 and 0.90. The value of 0.90 reflectance is considered to be unlikely high. Even 
with a highly reflective façade material of 0.95 in reflectance, the window façade area part of 20% 
will lower the weighted average reflectance of the façade down to 0.80. See calculations in Table 
3-20.

oriented apartments, where light is mainly reflected from the opposing south oriented façade. A 
façade reflectance value between 0.30 and 0.60 are considered realistic for the existing conditions 
and 0.60 could be the case with new high reflecting façade cladding.

Table 3-22. A possible explanation is that balconies are obstructing more of the view to the sky,

It has to be said that the façade reflectance of each building are influencing each other, whereas 
lower façade reflectance for one façade will influence the effect of the other façade as well, as the 
received light is a sum of both direct light from the sky as well as reflected light. 
Simulations show that the façade reflectance additionally influence the DF within the canopy 
noticeably at the measured height of 0.80 m above the canopy ground. A reduction of  9 % and an 
increase of 12 % are found for the two variations compared to the REF simulation with façade 
reflectance of 0.60.

Figure 3-82 Illustration of light reflections from a high 
reflective north oriented façade. 
 
A highly reflective cladding could 
be installed at the north oriented 
façade giving the needed light for 
the south oriented rooms. Glare 
issues are considered to be limited, 
as direct solar radiation seldom 
occurs on the north oriented façade. 
A cladding protected by the canopy 
can be chosen with higher freedom 
as it doesn’t have to withstand the 
rough weather conditions. It is 
likely that a façade protected by the 
canopy will keep its reflectance for 
longer than a surface exposed to 
weather conditions. 
A mirroring façade could give a feeling of a wider and less enclosed space within the canopy, giving 
an interesting experience of the space inside the canopy.  
A high contrast ratio between the facades and the canopy covering could lead to eye strains. This 
contrast ratio would be reduced with higher façade reflectance.  
4.4.10 Canopy framing geometry 
Frames are needed in order to hold the canopy envelope in place. The frames are considered to be 
important for the canopy design of several reasons. Besides less material usage, smaller frames will 
allow more daylight to pass through and have architectural benefits as the construction will be 
experienced as less enclosed and more in contact with the outside. 
Former investigations have been done without including framing geometry for the canopy, as both 
the framing and construction system is not yet known. The chosen light transmittance of 0.70 for 
the REF simulation takes the framing into account. Nevertheless, it is a simplification. A framing 
system can be constructed in numerous ways; width, height, form and reflectance of the frames 
influence how the light is propagating through the construction. Furthermore, the distance between 
the frames has a major effect and the framing system can be varying with fewer load bearing beams 
with smaller frames in between.  
This section involve simulations performed with simple frame geometries included. As the frames 
are now included in the simulation geometry, the light transmittance of the transparent surfaces 
between the frames are increased to 0.85. The simplified frame geometries are not considered to be 
valid construction proposals, but are thought to be basis for discussion that can help guide the 
construction geometry in the early design phase regarding daylight.  
Two sets of simulations are performed, each set with two different frame heights of 250 mm and 
500 mm. One set is simulated with frames spanning across the canopy, from one building to the 
other and the other set is simulated with frames spanning along the building direction. The latter 
should be seen as a hypothetical design solution showing the influence of frames going in this 
crossed direction. A more complex framing geometry is expected for the final EKO-Canopy design. 
Specific setup of the simulation geometries is further described in the following. 
==PAGE:99==
4.4 - Daylight 
Page 97 
 
 
Figure 3-83 South elevation section of frames across with 500 mm height. Distance between all frames are set 
to 2000 mm and the frame width set to 200 mm. Frame height and frame orientation of the upper frames are 
differing in other simulations, while the vertical frames does not change orientation. See details at Figure 3-87 
and Figure 3-85 
 
Figure 3-84 Frame measures for 500 mm frame heights.  
 
Figure 3-85 Frame measures for 250 mm frame heights. Reflectance for all the frames are set to 0.70 LT 
The measures of the framing system will cover (100% * 2m/0.2m) = 10 % of the canopy surface 
area, but the framing height will have additional effect on the DF as well as the view through the 
construction, when seen from angled directions. 
Each of the four simulation geometries are illustrated and described in the following. The sun angle 
is set to be at equinox the 21st March 10 am on all illustrations with shadows appearing on the 
ground and on the south oriented facades. Perspectives are seen from the suns angle the same date 
later on the day at 4 pm.

When seen from the shown 
perspective, the 500 mm frames 
spanning across appears to be non-
transparent. The overlapping effect 
from the framing construction is 
considered to be important for the 
visual comfort of the canopy.  
Frames spanning across is assessed to 
give better view to the sky from 
within the apartments, which is 
considered of great importance. This 
is further described in (4.2.2)  
 
Figure 3-87 Frames across 250 mm height 
 
Frames with 250mm height allow 
transparency when seen from the 
chosen perspective. A similar effect 
can be achieved by larger distance 
between the frames.  
Projected shadows on the ground 
and on the facades seems more 
elegant. With the existing uniform 
building facades, a vivid play with 
shadows can be used to make the 
perception of the space more 
interesting.  
 
Figure 3-88 Frames along 500 mm height 
 
500 mm frames spanning along the 
building facades, results in better 
transparency when seen from one of 
the canopy ends. However the 
orientation blocks the view to the sky 
from within the apartments. 
Projected shadows on the façade will 
follow the longitudinal lines in the 
façade, making the long facades look 
even longer, which can be considered 
to give a less interesting experience of 
the canopy space than what can be 
achieved with more dynamic shades.

along the buildings, give a relatively 
transparent experience from the 
chosen perspective and allow 
significantly more transparency seen 
from the lower located apartments, 
but still blocks a relatively large part 
the sky.

Although the orientation of the frames does not seem to have a critical influence on the DF within 
the apartments, the visual experience is important to consider. Frames spanning across will in 
general allow better view from the apartments.  
The intension of the framing analysis is to make an initial basis to guide the further framing design. 
A glass roof design is likely to be similar to the one at Bovieran which is constructed with long truss 
beams spanning across with a crossing framing system holding the glass (see 2.1.1 - Bovieran ). An 
alternative construction using ETFE cushions instead of glass open up possibilities for alternate 
designs as the ETFE material are much more light and flexible (see 2.4.4 - ETFE). 
In order to minimize the frame construction a wire frame system inspired by the Khan Satyr tent 
construction could be considered. (See 0 -  
ETFE constructions) Elegant examples of combined wires and steel tubes are also found to have 
very high level of transparency as the example in Figure 3-92. 
 
 
Figure 3-92 Structure example from Vector Foiltec: TEXLON 41 meters long steel frame/wire structure 
(Baseller Platz, Germany) The ETFE cushions are about 3.4 m wide. Upper steel beam consists of 127 mm 
tubes with 76 mm diagonal tubes linking together the upper beams with the wires below, with diameters from 
just 14 – 26 mm. Source: (Gmbh 2015) 
In case wood is considered used in the construction possible larger beam dimensions should be 
considered along with the relatively low light reflectance of wood. The Waitomo Glowworm Caves 
visitor centre elegantly uses wood in combination with wires, see (0 -  
ETFE constructions). 
When a construction system is chosen, further daylight studies can help to optimize the daylight 
conditions for the final design completing the integrated design process.

It is seen that the north oriented rooms within the canopy are the most influenced by the added 
building mass with a reduction of 19 %. A significant reduction of 7 % is found in the south 
oriented rooms within the canopy. This can be explained by a reduction of light being reflected on 
the opposing north oriented façade. The simulation does not show how much of the reduced 
daylight is coursed by the added building mass at the top compared to the mass added at the gables. 
But the simulations show that additional building mass can have critical influence of the DF within 
the critical located apartments. 
In case additional building mass is wanted, additional studies can be made. Expansion in the 
building length is assumed to have less influence on the daylight than expansion in the height.

4.5 
Conclusion of daylight investigations 
Existing Dragonvägen apartments are found to have relatively high Window-to-floor-area-ratios 
between 15-37 %. This is well above the minimum of 10 % WFR according to both the SS 914201  
and Danish Building Regulations as well as the Norwegian TEK10 building regulations. The large 
windows are mainly located at the south oriented rooms facing the balconies. However, the balcony 
overhangs of 1.75 m combined with the balcony side walls result in significantly lower DF 
conditions than what is found for the north oriented rooms.  
Simulations show that a considerable part of the existing rooms achieves DF noticeably below the 
SS 914201  recommendation of having at least 1.0 %DF 1m from at the darkest sidewall at half the 
room depth. The first two examples in Table 3-25 are based on existing conditions for apartments at 
1st floor. The first one without balcony glazing and the second with balcony glazing included. A 
reduction in DF of more than 30% is simulated for the balcony glazing, resulting in DF values far 
below the recommended 1.0 %DF.  
Simulations of apartments at the upper 7th floor show about 50 % higher DF results than what is 
found for the critically located apartments at 1st floor. 
Table 3-25 Selected simulation result output for south oriented rooms. Grid point values according to SS 
914201  are included on the ISO contour images. Deviation in DF compared to existing condition are 
included for the last simulation results. Simulation result values smaller than 1.0 %DF are indicated with red 
text. 
Existing conditions

The balcony glazing is assumed to be removed in a case where an EKO-Canopy covering is 
installed. Simulations show better DF results for a situation with a clear 3-layer ETFE canopy 
construction than what is achieved for existing conditions with balcony glazing.  
Variations with light transmittance of the canopy showed DF deviations roughly corresponding to 
the light transmittance, meaning 0.70 LT result in daylight factors of roughly 70 % of what is 
achieved. Deviations are assumed to be caused by the angle of incidence of the incoming light.  
Ground reflectance was found to have a considerable influence on the DF within the 1st floor 
apartments. However, luxuriant vegetation and attractive views within the canopy is considered to 
be of high importance, although lower light reflectance will be present.  
Examples of framing constructions ware investigated for different orientations and frame heights. 
Frames spanning across the canopy space ware found to give best daylight conditions for 
apartments. The view through the construction was considered to be an additional advantage for 
this orientation.  
In case a highly reflective façade cladding is installed at the north façade, an average reflectance of 
more than 0.80 can be achieved, when the low reflectance from windows are included. The shown 
results for façade reflectance of 0.90 are considered to be unrealistically high. However, only a

4.6 - Daylight
