portion of the gains from the higher façade reflectance seem to improve the DF conditions 
considerably.  
Simulations with thicker walls representing a situation with renovated walls, show more than 20 % 
reduction of north oriented rooms and about 7-8 % reduction for south oriented rooms.  
In general the studies shows that it is possible to improve daylight conditions for the most critical 
rooms compared to existing conditions when existing balcony glazing is taken into account. The 
new canopy space shows daylight conditions that can be considered at exceptionally high for an 
indoor space with a Daylight Factor ranging between 40-60 %DF.  
4.6 
Discussion of daylight investigations 
The SS 914201 standard of 1.0 %DF is not met for existing conditions. This form a good basis to 
discuss alternative strategies to secure good daylight conditions for the Dragonvägen buildings and 
similar million program buildings. A well-lit EKO-Canopy can be a way to compensate for the poor 
daylight conditions within the apartments.  
It has to be taken into account that the investigations are focussing on the most critically located 
apartment rooms. This can give the assumption of worse daylight conditions than what applies for 
the majority of the apartments.  
4.6.1 
Daylight in canopy 
In a successful execution of an EKO-Canopy project, people will spend more of their time within 
the well-lit canopy. With conditioned climate and daylight conditions close to matching outdoor 
conditions, the space intend to invite people to spend more of their time within the canopy in 
favour of some of the hours they usually spend indoors with considerable lower amount of daylight. 
A construction that allow plenty of daylight to pass is important to achieve good daylight conditions. 
A canopy construction with ETFE covering can allow more of the daylight wavelengths to pass 
through, including wavelengths in the UV-spectrum. This can even allow occupants to get a sun tan 
at the balconies within seasons that would normally be considered too cold.  
Snowfall will result in high ground reflectance during wintertime, whereas the canopy can have a 
tendency to be covered or partly covered with snow which will decrease daylight conditions instead 
of improving them during the winter time with most critical daylight conditions. Limiting the 
covering of snow during winter time can be important to secure acceptable daylight conditions 
within the dark season. That said, there seems to be interesting ways to light up ETFE constructions 
with artificial lighting, which could contribute to the experience of the space in winter time (see 0 -  
ETFE constructions). 
Although acceptable daylight conditions can be achieved within the concerning apartments, an 
important aspect to be answered is if the view from the apartments can be accepted, with an EKO-
Canopy covering.  
Although outdoor daylight conditions can be considered to be a more important aspect of “being 
able following outdoor weather conditions” than experiencing weather changes of precipitation, the 
precipitation is still a part of the outdoor environment that will not be experienced in the same way 
for rooms facing the canopy. However, new luxuriant vegetation can be seen as new benefits that 
are likely to characterize an EKO-Canopy space, improving the views from apartment windows. 
4.6.2 
Design approaches 
Based on the studies of façade reflectance influence on daylight, an idea of installing a façade 
cladding with mirroring properties is suggested to replace the out-dated existing façade cladding on 
==PAGE:108==
4.6 - Daylight 
Page 106 
 
the north façade giving the much-needed light for the south oriented rooms. The facades inside the 
canopy are protected by the rough weather conditions, including wind loads, precipitation and frost. 
Therefore, a cladding material can be chosen with greater freedom, likely a significantly cheaper 
cladding with considerably cheaper attachment system.  
It is expected that a façade protected by the canopy will keep its reflectance for longer than a surface 
exposed to weather conditions. Glare issues are considered to be limited, as direct solar radiation 
seldom occurs on the north oriented façade. Additionally, a mirroring façade can give an interesting 
architectural appearance, and remove the focus from the windows to the reflections of the façade, 
giving more privacy for the residents. A mirroring façade could give a feeling of a wider and less 
enclosed space within the canopy.  
A highly reflective façade could additionally reduce the contrast ratio between the light canopy 
covering and the facades, giving more desirable daylight conditions within the canopy.  
Participation in the closing event of “International year of Light 2015” the 15th of December 2015 at 
The Black Diamond, Copenhagen, lead to a more open minded examination of the daylight 
investigations, as lectures from Olafur Elliason and Tor Nørretranders among others gave examples 
of how to design with light. 2015 was nominated as the “International year of Light” by UNESCO.  
In Germany there are increasingly more projects that plays with reflecting daylight into atriums with 
the use of heliostats, which are computer controlled mirrors that ensure reflected sunlight on a 
specified target as the sun moves across the sky. The technology has been installed at Genzyme 
Center at Cambridge, but the technology is expensive and should only be used when the daylight 
design is not sufficient. (Auer 2015) (Vorlesung 4) 
4.6.3 
Dirt and condensation on windows 
Dirt on windows result in lower light transmission as described in (2.3.2.2 - Dirt influence on 
transparency). Figure 3-12 shows that double layer residential windows with a light transmittance of 
75-85% get the light transmittance reduced to 65-75% in a “dirty” condition and down to 50-60% 
when considered as “very dirty (but possible to look through)”.  
It is expected that windows covered by the canopy is less exposed to dirt, resulting in a generally 
higher light transmittance. This effect is assumed to be mostly influencing windows on the north 
façade which are not protected by balconies. However, the inside of balconies is likely to be kept 
cleaner resulting in higher reflectance. 
Horizontal windows seem to be more affected by dirt. With a self-cleaning EFTE canopy, this issue 
is considered to be reduced significantly. However, ETFE require another challenge to be solved, 
which is surface condensation, reducing light and especially the visual transparency. This challenge is 
likely to be solved by blowing warmer air on the inside surface of the canopy, which is further 
described in (0 - % RH EKO-Canopy ventilation strategy) 
4.6.4 
Method considerations 
It has to be taken into account that the influence of the parameter variations is likely to deviate from 
the results, if other parameters are deviating considerably from the reference setup. As basis the 
reference reflectance values are set relatively high, with the assumption that an EKO-Canopy will be 
designed with focus on good daylight conditions. However, reflectance of existing conditions is 
included for each simulation where the deviation of the design approaches is in focus. 
It was considered to only include rooms facing the canopy in the investigations and comparing 
results with simulation without the canopy. This could have made the studies more simple to assess 
for the reader, as the additional information can be distracting. Nevertheless, it was chosen to 
==PAGE:109==
4.6 - Daylight 
Page 107 
 
include the reference rooms for each simulation to achieve the additional results for further analysis 
and as a method to continuously check results for possible unwanted deviations. The simulations 
with changed façade and ground reflectance as well as the simulation with added balcony glazing 
and added wall thickness provide information about how these parameters influence both the 
existing apartments as well as the apartments influenced by the canopy.  
Using the SS 914201 standard where two specific points has to be assessed in each room is 
considered to be a reasonable way to assess the DF to take the distribution of daylight into account. 
Assessment with average DF used in the Norwegian standard and the Active House certification 
system resulted in significantly higher values for north oriented rooms than the south oriented 
rooms, as the high DF in front of the windows has a major influence on the average values.   
The BR15 method used in Denmark and in DGNB is considered to be representing the both the 
daylight amount and distribution in a reasonable way that include more information than the SS 
914201. Extended grid points could have been included in the grid point file to get values, however 
the ISO contour images are included for further assessment. 
Although the simulations have been carefully setup with numerous test simulations to get the most 
reliable results and to understand how the VDV software calculate, the simulation results should 
only be considered as guiding. Material properties and light transmittance are based on assumptions 
and the software includes uncertainties to some extent.  
==PAGE:110==
5.1 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 108 
 
5 
Energy performance and indoor climate 
5.1 
Introduction 
To investigate how an EKO-Canopy will influence energy performance and indoor climate for 
existing apartments, various designs and strategies are investigated in this chapter. An EKO-Canopy 
is expected to reduce the heating demand of apartments, due to a reduced heat loss through the 
building envelope facing the canopy. 
In order to maintain good thermal indoor climate conditions, room heating is needed, at times 
where internal gains are not sufficient.  
5.1.1 
Energy design overview 
A building’s energy performance and indoor climate are results of dynamic external conditions, 
internal gains, and properties of the building envelope as well as performance of building services. 
An overview of the heat balance components is illustrated in Figure 3-95. 
Apartments are actively heated by radiators in addition to internal gains from occupants, equipment 
and solar gains through windows. The canopy is passively heated by the solar gains and conduction 
through apartment outer walls. Air exchanges between the canopy and apartments depend on the 
mechanical ventilation system. In the case where mechanical ventilation is installed, parts of the 
intake air will be preheated through the canopy. 
 
Figure 3-95 Bioclimatic drawing showing basic canopy heat balance. Blue arrows show ventilation and 
infiltration, red arrows show heat conduction. The illustration is a simplification showing dominating heat 
balances for a simple canopy design.  
Heat losses from buildings occur when outdoor temperatures are lower than indoor temperatures 
and increases in relation with temperature difference. Heat losses and gains are the main concerns of 
investigations; more accurately described as heat transfer, in the form of conduction, convection and 
radiation, as well as ventilation loss caused by infiltration and natural ventilation. Heat gains are 
==PAGE:111==
5.2 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 109 
 
mainly internal gains from occupants, equipment etc. and solar gains through windows and EKO-
Canopy.  
The general heat transfer principles are interesting in relation to the Canopy’s potential for reducing 
external conduction and decreasing ventilation losses, by accumulating heat from solar gains and 
external conduction to the EKO-Canopy space. In addition, it is investigated whether the benefits 
of the EKO-Canopy can be compromised by overheating during summer. 
Table 3-26 Swedish naming for ventilation systems are given as follows (Eskilsson 2015)

Evaluation of thermal Indoor environment 
For apartments, overheating is evaluated in relation to the Danish Building Regulations. Residential 
buildings must not to exceed indoor operative temperatures above 27 °C for more than 100 hours 
and 28 °C for 25 hours per year (BR15 2015) (§6 (Trafik- og Byggestyrelsen, 2015)

5.2.1.1 
Wind chill factor 
Higher air velocities decrease the thermal surface resistance on human skin. This results in a lower 
sensed temperature. In outdoor conditions, there are various models of how to include wind 
velocities within the sensed temperature. In order to find a way to assess the effect of the sheltered 
canopy space, a comparison of the outdoor temperature based on wind chill factor has been 
calculated. eq (3-5) show the Lenntech model to calculated wind chill factor. (Source: lenntech.com) 
Tapparent(°C) = 33 + (Tair- 33)*(0.474 + 0.454√(v)-0.0454*v) 
eq (3-5) 
Wind chill factor can be considered (wind speeds close to relatively high buildings vs. people find 
places with less wind -> just use wind from data file) (wind chill factor could be multiplied to the 
outside air temperature and compared to the temperature in the canopy with the anticipated internal 
air movements) 
 
Figure 3-98 Stockholm annual averaged outdoor temperatures with wind chill factor  
The calculation is a simplification, as addressed in (3.2 - Canopy wind load investigations) wind 
conditions at the occupied areas around the Dragonvägen buildings changes according to wind 
direction and the surrounding buildings. For wind directions coming from north and south, the 
wind seemed to be reduced significantly, and for wind coming from east and west increased wind 
speeds was found. The calculations are meant as a rough estimate to consider the influence of the 
canopy’s wind sheltering effects. See analysis in (Appendix Part III E.2a)

5.3 
IESVE existing conditions (Dragonvägen) 
5.3.1 
Setup of simulation  
Setup of the model is based on (PART 1:1.4.2 - Dragonvägen - case study) IES-VE is used for 
dynamic simulation of energy and indoor environment.  
The modelled IESVE geometry of Dragonvägen existing conditions is shown at Figure 3-99. 
 
 
Figure 3-99 IESVE model geometry of existing condition of Dragonvägen. Shading surfaces are shown with 
green colour. Building components facing outside are shown with purple colour, with transparency on 
windows. Internal surfaces are grey. 
As described in (PART 1:1.4.3 Figure 1-7) there are about 8% of the of the Miljonprogramme 
apartment houses that has been renovated with additional insulation in the roof. Some of these 
renovations have included new penthouses, which is the case for Dragonvägen. As the studies also 
seeks to represent the general EKO-Canopy concept, it is chosen to simplify the investigations by 
combining the penthouse with the basement floor, and simulated the basement as an additional 
apartment floor. Furthermore, it has been difficult to find sufficient information of the basement 
and the penthouses.  
The geometry is modelled with external measures, therefore the floor area within the simulation 
model is larger than the 6700 m2 found for the Dragonvägen buildings. This is considered in the 
simulation setup and results are based on the floor area of the Dragonvägen buildings. Internal stair 
cases are simulated as being a part of the heated floor area.  
Shadings surfaces are included for opposing building blocks as shown in Figure 3-99. Balcony 
overhangs and side fins are included as well.  
5.3.1.1 
Thermal constructions setup 
Constructions are based on (PART 1:1.4.2 - Table 1-3 Description of Dragonvägen building 
envelope. Selected information from (Ab & Ritningar 2013). (Translated from Swedish). 
Existing windows are found to be double layer glazing with air filling. Windows are installed with 40 
mm wooden frames with an average window part of 13%.

5.3.1.5 
Natural ventilation 
Natural ventilation within residential buildings is difficult to predict. A simplification of natural 
ventilation within the apartments are selected. An air change of 5 ACH is setup with a ramp 
function from 23.5 °C to 27 °C where the ventilation will each 5 ACH.

To convert results from the blower door test to usable input for IES-VE, the infiltration has to be 
given in ACH and converted from the 50 Pa pressure to normal pressure. Furthermore, infiltration 
==PAGE:117==
5.3 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 115 
 
due to use of the building should be included. Stair cases in the building are placed internally, which 
is anticipated to work as a storm porch that reduces air infiltration when the main door is used. The 
main infiltration due to usage of the building is anticipated to be from opening of windows for 
natural ventilation and opening of balcony doors for access to balconies. The specific usage of the 
buildings is difficult to estimate. 
To give a qualified estimate on the infiltration during occupation, a calculation method from SBi 213 
Page 58-59 (Aggerholm & Grau 2008) is used. Natural ventilation, opening of windows and doors 
as well as mechanical exhaust is included as infiltration in the calculation for occupied hours. This 
correspond to the ventilation principal of the existing building.  
The expression for occupied hours is used for the  
The infiltration pr. m2 heated floor areas in occupied hours is calculated as follows: 
Infiltration = 0.04 + 0.06 · q50 [l/s · m2] 
eq (3-6) 
And outside occupied hours as; 
Infiltration = 0.06 · q50 [l/s · m2] 
eq (3-7) 
Only volumes and envelope areas are given in the blower door tests. In order to use the formulas 
from SBi 213, the leakage is converted from envelope area to floor are. By using the floor height of 
2.4 m:  
Infiltration = 0.04 + 0.06 · 0.392 l/s · m2 = 0.0635 l/s · m2  
This can be converted to air change pr. hour by multiplying with the ratio between (n50 / q50):  
ACH = Infiltration · (n50 / q50) = 0.0635 l/s · m2 · (1.785h-1 / 0.392 l/s · m2) = (See table Figure 
3-102) 
 
Figure 3-102 Calculated infiltration values based on SBi 213 Page 58-59 (Aggerholm & Grau 2008) and on 
blower door and floor height of 2.45. See appendix for  
Apartment front doors are facing the common stair cases placed inside the building. Air exchanges 
with outdoor air due to this is considered to be limited. However, balconies are available for all 
apartments with balcony doors that open directly to the outside.  
5.3.1.7 
Electricity and DHW (Domistic Hot Water) 
No information is available of how much of the district heating that is used for DHW consumption 
in Dragonvägen. An indication about the DHW usage can be seen in the district heating 
consumption during the warm summer months, where heating should be turned off (See Appendix 
Part I B.2 – Annual energy consumption). The average district heating consumption for June, July 
and August is 21.3 MWh/month. If this is considered to be used every month during the year as 
DHW, a total of 38 kWh/m2 is found. However, BE15 show an annual consumption of only 21.1 
kWh/m2 for residential apartment buildings. A compromised of value of 30 kWh/m2 is assumed. 
The DHW is added to the simulation results tables to calculate total annual energy consumption.

5.4 
IESVE Basic canopy  
The IESVE Canopy model geometry can be seen on Figure 3-109. The canopy area is modelled 
with 8 zones to include adequate simulation of stratification of air according to IESVE user guide 
on how to simulate atriums with MacroFlo (iesve.com). The relatively high canopy room of 22 m is 
considered to include considerable stratification effect of the air inside the canopy. 
The basic canopy include thermal zones between the two existing Dragonvägen buildings. In this 
situation, thermal conduction at the internal facades are exchanged with the canopy thermal zones. 
 
Figure 3-107 IESVE Canopy model geometry with 8 zones used for Simulation 4-9. Shading surfaces are 
shown with green colour. Building components facing outside are shown with purple colour, with 
transparency on windows. Internal surfaces are shown with grey colour. 
5.4.1 
ETFE canopy 
ETFE is not typical construction element to be analysed for dynamic building simulations. There are 
several aspects that makes the material challenging to perform correct in a simulation program. This 
include: 
• 
A deviating light transmission spectre  
• 
Relatively large cavities between layers 
• 
Cushion form makes the material surfaces bend 
• 
Alternative shading technology  
• 
Low radiant temperature from caused by the thin layers of ETFE 
• 
Deviating surface  
• 
Allow UV and Infrared light -> heat + sun tan + radiation from canopy to night sky 
Table 2 Heat fluxes and temperatures for a simplified glass and ETFE model (for 
both cases direct solar transmittance (Tdir) of 6% was assumed)

Theory about how to simulate with ETFE is considered in accordance to “Energy modelling of 
ETFE membranes in building applications” (Poirazis et al. 2009).  
Setup of 3-layer ETFE construction is shown in Figure 3-108. The material properties are setup in 
accordance to the data sheet of the TEXLON system by Vector Foiltec found in (Appendix Part I 
B.3) This include a U-value of 3-layer ETFE construction of 1.76 W/m2K and 1.26 W/m2K for 4-
layer ETFE and g-values of 0.77 and 0.72 respectively.

5.4.2 
Ventilation 
5.4.2.1 
Infiltration  
The intension is to let the canopy be used day and night as well as summer and winter. Many people 
are expected to enter and leave the building both summer and winter as well as day and night. This 
require a leak in the construction that results in an unwanted air change without heat recovery. A 
door that is only open when used is crucial to reduce the air change, therefore it is assumed that a 
good solution is chosen, very likely a storm porch/draft lobby. A basic infiltration of 0.1 ACH is 
chosen all year around corresponding to 4702 m3/h 
==PAGE:123==
5.4 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 121 
 
5.4.2.2 
Mechanical ventilation 
The setup uses the average temperature of the canopy zones. Temperatures within the canopy zones 
are deviating in the simulations which is also expected due to stratification of the air. However, the 
air inside the canopy are expected to be mixed actively, likely with ceiling fans as seen on the 
Bovieran building (2.1.1 - Bovieran – Swedish housing concept).  
IESVE Profile: Apartment auxiliary intake air, setup for whole year 
0.5*to+0.5*(avg(ta(1),ta(2),ta(3),ta(4),ta(5),ta(6),ta(7),ta(8))) 
Another reason to use average temperatures is to avoid a complicated setup with many specific 
profiles as risks of making errors is considerably higher if the number of profiles within the IESVE 
model gets too high.  
 
5.4.2.3 
Natural ventilation in hot season 
 
Figure 3-110 Natural ventilation during hot season -  
Fresh air is sucked in through 
apartments by a chimney effect in 
the canopy as seen on Figure 3-110. 
However, this is a simplified model. 
More detailed simulations are 
performed with IESVE MacroFlo. 
Openings are considered to be 
mechanically controlled based on 
the indoor temperature in the 
occupied zone in the canopy. 
 
IESVE profile for natural ventilation 
ramp(ta,23.5,0,26,1) 
 
Investigations of various MacroFlo openings has been performed, see Figure 3-111. The blue graph 
show a case where only the roof have openings for natural ventilation, which result in extreme 
overheating temperatures within the month of June. Ventilation openings at both the lower levels  
and roof seems to be the most efficient setup, using the smallest opening areas. All other variations 
include openings at both roof and ground floors. The chosen setup is shown in Figure 3-112. 
 
Figure 3-111 IESVE simulations with various setup of MacroFlo openings. (The volume flows can be seen in 
Appendix Part III E.2e) 
==PAGE:124==
5.4 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 122 
 
 
Figure 3-112 Setup of natural ventilation openings in canopy for IESVE MacroFlo 
5.4.3 
Ground 
Heat exchange between ground and the canopy depend on the temperature difference between the 
canopy and the ground temperature as well as the thermal properties at the ground. The relatively 
large ground surface of 3791 m2, constituted by the two existing buildings and the canopy (2*871 m2 
+ 2050 m2), will heat up the underlying ground over time.  
A relatively large part of the canopy boundary conditions is the ground. The canopy covers an area 
of 2177 m2. When the Dragonvägen buildings of 12 m in depth are included, a total of 3918 m2 are 
covered (72.56 m x 54 m). With the shortest distance to the centre of 27 m, the outdoor weather 
conditions have a relatively small direct influence on the ground temperature below the canopy.  
Temperature fluctuations occur mainly in the upper layers of the ground, where air temperatures 
and radiation affect the ground surface temperature. Depending on soil type and annual weather 
conditions the ground temperature fluctuations are present down to about 10 m below the ground 
surface where it can be seen as constant during the year. (Florides & Kalogirou 2005) 
The major part of the canopy ground is facing the ground below the adjacent two buildings.  
 
Figure 3-113 Illustration of ground  
It is not known to what extend other 
buildings in the area are influencing 
the ground temperature.  
To include the influence of the 
ground in the simulations, 10 meters 
of soil is included in the canopy 
ground. An adjacent temperature of 
6.2 °C corresponding to the annual 
average temperature in Stockholm. 
The transmission is one dimensional, 
whereas the boundaries are simulated 
as adiabatic.  
 
The default soil type in IESVE is used with following properties: 
c = 1250 J/kg K  
ρ = 1800 kg/m3 
λ  = 1.3 W/m2K  
==PAGE:125==
5.4 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 123 
 
The λ-value is smaller than values suggested in (DS/EN ISO 13370 2008) (Table 1 – Thermal 
properties of the ground), however the standard suggest to model the ground with a depth equal to 
the width of the building, which in this case would be 54 m. The combination of less ground 
thickness and smaller λ-value is considered to be a conservative assumption.  
Thermal inertia/resistance                     
 
The large volume with relatively high thermal inertia result in a high temperature rigidity.   
1709 J/K*m2*s-1 
A test simulation with a preconditioning temperature of 10°C and 365 days of preconditioning 
period is performed. In order to reduce simulation time, test simulations with various 
preconditioning temperatures has been performed to avoid the long preconditioning period. 
IESVE Preconditioning period set to one whole year 365 days 
 
Figure 3-114 External heat conduction from canopy ground thermal zone. Three different simulations are 
shown 1) A whole year simulated initially with an initial ground temperature of 10 °C 2) Simulated with 3 days 
precondition and initial ground temperature of 13 °C 3) Simulated with an adiabatic ground boundary. 
The simulation with 13 °C preconditioning temperature was found to be a good conservative match 
to the simulation setup with 10°C preconditioning temperature and 365 days preconditioning time.  
Figure 3-113 show simulation results that indicate considerable grins from the ground. The figure 
include a simulation with adiabatic boundaries at the ground, resulting in negative heat gains of 2-3 
MWh each month of the year, which is heat losses through the canopy sidewalls. The results show 
that the ground have a positive influence on the temperatures during the whole year, as it works as 
an efficient thermal mass with long continuation, accumulating heat during the hot periods and 
==PAGE:126==
5.4 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 124 
 
delivering heat during the cold periods, even until February where the heat seems to be submitted. If 
the negative gains form the simulation of adiabatic ground is taken into account, as being the 
heating losses through the canopy façade, the simulation results show that event februeay has a 
positive heat gain to the ground canopy zone. 
5.4.4 
Basic canopy solar shading 
 
During the IESVE analysis process, it was found that 
the northern building block received about 30 % more 
solar gains during summer time when the canopy was 
added. Solar gains ware assumed to be reduced within 
the apartments when covered by the canopy.  
As solar gains in winter had a realistic reduction of 
about 30 % comparing to the g-value, it is assumed 
that the reason for the misleading results is connected 
to a bug in IESVE. Test simulations indicate that the 
explanation is connected with IESVE SunCast, which 
does not seem to include internal shading in the solar 
gain SunCast calculations, resulting in higher solar 
gains. 
All kind of variations has been tested to find a 
solution to this (See Appendix Part III E.1e). The best 
solution was found to be to exclude the “Calculate 
diffuse shading factors” for the SunCast calculations. 
This resulted in more realistic distribution of daylight 
but resulted in overall higher solar gains. The solar 
gains ware corrected by changing the g-value for all 
windows from 0.80 to 0.55. The result is seen at 
Figure 3-115 
 
 
==PAGE:127==
5.4 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 125 
 
 
Figure 3-115 Annual solar gains at north and south located buildings. Including three simulations: 1) 
Simulation with diffuse shading included 2) Simulation without diffuse shading included 3) Simulation without 
diffuse shading & adjusted g-values on both internal and external windows.  
 
 
Figure 3-116 Solar gains in summer (With diffuse solar shading on) High solar radiation have most effect on 
North oriented rooms – indicating that solar shading is not included at the balconies

5.4.5 
Results - basic canopy 
 
Figure 3-117 Sim4 - Basic canopy with 3-layer ETFE.  
Figure 3-117 show results of a basic canopy configuration without further technical applications. 
The heating needs are reduced to 65 % of the existing conditions. Large savings are seen on external 
conduction and heating losses due to mechanical ventilation. The temperatures within the canopy is 
considerable higher than outdoor temperatures, especially when compared to the calculated wind 
chill factor calculated outdoor temperatures. The operative temperatures are found to be critically 
higher than the air temperature, with a maximum operative temperature of 36 °C and a maximum 
air temperature of 29 °C. The relative humidity is shown to be considerably lower than the outdoor 
humidity. With 4663 hours being between 30-50% RH and 1094 between 10-30 % RH and 16 hours 
below critical 10 % RH EKO-Canopy ventilation strategy.

5.5 
Canopy with heat recovery ventilation strategy 
 
Canopy with ventilation strategy 
• 
Heat exchanger on apartment exhaust 
air recovers heat for canopy supply air 
(F(T)X) 
• 
(5) 50/50 supply air: canopy/outside 
• 
(6) Variation: 100 % from canopy 
 
 
 
 
Ventilation strategy for heating season. Exhaust air from apartments is used to preheat intake air for 
canopy by an air-to-air heat exchanger.  
Lane, about Shan Shatyr: 
“In winter, a key challenge is to prevent the formation of ice on the inside of the envelope; this is achieved by a 
combination of temperature control and directing warm air currents up the inner surface of the fabric, a strategy 
that also prevents downdraughts” (Lane 2010) 
 
 
Figure 3-118 Sim – 5 Canopy (3-layer ETFE) with heat recovered air from apartments. Fan power is set to 
150 % of existing conditions, meaning 50% increase.

5.6.1 
Canopy with directed supply air  
Canopy with additional renovation 
• 
Heat exchanger on apartment exhaust 
air recovers heat for canopy supply air 
(FTX) 
• 
100 % supply from canopy 
• 
Additional insulation: 
• 
+350 mm insulation 
• 
New windows 1.2 W/m2K 
 
All supply air for apartments is considered to come from the canopy when indoor temperatures are 
<25°C. When >25°C 75% of air comes from outside and 25% comes from canopy 
Auxiliary inlet in apartments 
if(ta<25,avg(ta(1),ta(2),ta(3),ta(4),ta(5),ta(6),ta(7),ta(8)),0.75*to+0.25*(avg(ta(1),ta(2),ta(3),ta(4),ta(5),t 
a(6),ta(7),ta(8)))) 
The system setup can be seen as an ideal situation where all facades facing away from the canopy is 
sealed during heating season. This configuration will not only require sealing of the facades. It is 
likely that the south facades with balconies allow significantly more air to get in than what is found 
for the north oriented facades. However, no data is available and as the simulation is including one 
of each facade, it is considered as an acceptable simplification to simulate the building blocks 
similarly. It is not known how the system should work precisely, or how to secure sufficient air 
within the rooms that are not facing the canopy. Figure 3-120 shows a possible scenario.

The additional layer of ETFE result in a 2.4 kWh/m2 reduction in heating needs for the concerning 
configuration. The initial simulations with 3 and 4 layers of ETFE showed to have less influence on 
the heating needs by only going down from 53.3 kWh/m2 to 51.4 kWh/m2. This can be explained 
with the configuration of sim9 where 100% supply air for the apartments comes preheated from the 
canopy. 
The additional layer of ETFE show to have significant improvement on the temperatures within the 
canopy, with more hours within the category of 18-26 °C and less overheated hours. The additional 
layer allow less solar radiation to pass during hot days with the same setup of natural ventilation.

5.7 
Conclusion 
5.7.1 
Conclusion of energy performance 
An overview of the investigated renovation strategies is shown in Figure 3-124. The graph includes 
the annual energy balance for each of the simulations. For specific information about the 
simulations, see previous sections within this chapter. The simulations are setup chronological in the 
same way as addressed within the previous sections. 
 
 
Figure 3-124 Annual energy balance for investigated renovation strategies.  
Annual heating needs are included at the top of the columns in Figure 3-124. External conduction is 
found to be lowest for the two simulations at the right (8 and 9) representing configurations with 
both canopy and renovated external walls. However, the two simulations has higher internal gains 
than what is seen for the two renovated cases without canopy 
It is seen from Figure 3-124 that the solar gains are lower for the simulations including the canopy. 
In average the solar gains are 87 % lower for the simulations including the canopy. As only one 
façade one façade of each building faces the canopy this seems to be a realistic reduction. The 
TEXLON system has g-values of 0.72-0.76 (4-layer & 3-layer). An overview of the solar gains 
distribution over year can be seen in a 3d column diagram in Appendix Part III E.4a. 
 
 
==PAGE:135==
5.7 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 133 
 
 
Figure 3-125 Annual delivered energy for investigated renovation strategies. 
Figure 3-125 show the simulated heating needs combined with additional estimated energy 
consumptions for fans & pumps, lighting and DHW. Energy for “Fans & pumps” has been 
increased for the strategies with included heat recovery ventilation system. The scenario with 
conventional energy renovation has doubled the energy consumption form 6-12 kWh/m2, whereas 
the strategies that uses the canopy at supply air duct for the apartments is increased to only 9 
kWh/m2. Pressure losses in ducts are the main course of high fan power energy consumption.  
The strategy of using the canopy as supply air duct is assumed to result in significantly less pressure 
loss in ducts compared to what is found in conventional supply air duct systems. The lower energy 
consumption of fan power for the canopy configurations, results in a situation where sim8 and sim9 
nearly matches the conventional renovation sim3 with 85% heat recovery.  
With inclusion of primary energy factors of 2.5 for electricity and 0.8 for heating, strategy 8 and 9 
outperforms the conventional renovation 3. Figure 3-126 shows an overview of the primary energy 
usage for each of the scenarios.   
==PAGE:136==
5.7 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 134 
 
 
Figure 3-126 Annual primary energy consumption for investigated scenarios  
5.7.2 
Conclusion on thermal indoor climate  
The thermal indoor climate of the canopy has been in focus. The ground zone is considered to be 
the most important zone within the canopy as most people are assumed to be present. However, 
other zones are relevant for exchange of heat conduction and air exchanges between the canopy 
zones and apartments. Furthermore, the temperatures are relevant for the thermal environment at 
the balconies. 
 
Figure 3-127 The ground zone and the  
The top zone and ground zone has been chosen as focus for the discussion, as they represent the 
two most extreme conditions.  
Minimum, maximum and average air temperatures are shown in Table 3-30 for the canopy ground 
zone and the upper canopy zone as well as outdoor temperatures. Calculated Wind Chill outdoor 
temperatures based on Lenntech has been added to show how the actual outdoor wind conditions 
can be sensed in reality, whereas the canopy shelter for the wind and by that be experienced as 
warmer.  
==PAGE:137==
5.7 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 135 
 
The minimum air temperatures within the canopy are found to be significantly higher than the 
minimum outdoor temperatures. Minimum air temperatures are simulated to be about 2 °C colder 
in the upper canopy zone. In general the upper zone show to have higher oscillations than what is 
found for the ground zone. 
Maximum air temperatures for all simulations from 4-9 show to be very similar with largest 
deviations being less than a half degree C. Average air temperatures for the various canopy 
simulations also show similar results, with the largest deviation being the basic canopy 4 with no 
heat recovery configuration.  
Table 3-30 Air temperatures within canopy ground zone and upper canopy zone. 
 
Table 3-31 show the operative temperature that include background radiation. Significantly higher 
maximum temperatures are found for the canopy ground zone with maximum temperatures above 
35 °C for all canopy scenarios. Simulation 6 and 9 with 4 layer ETFE show the lowest maximum 
temperatures for the ground zone. This can be explained by the lower g-value for more ETFE layers 
along with the efficient natural ventilation. Both minimum and average operative temperatures for 
the two zones show to be close to the air temperatures.  
Table 3-31 Operative temperatures within canopy ground zone and upper canopy zone 
 
When comparing the outdoor temperatures with the temperatures within the canopy ground zone in 
Table 3-32 it is seen that considerably more hours are within acceptable temperature ranges, where 
the highest amount of hours for most of the simulations are between 18-26 °C. The outdoor 
conditions show most of the ours within the low temperature ranges.  
Table 3-32 Hours within range of operative temperatures, canopy occupied zone. 
 
Again the 4-layer ETFE show good performance on the thermal indoor climate evaluation, showing 
both to result in few hours with cold temperatures as well as few houses with very high 
temperatures. However, the canopy show to have too many hot hours for most simulations. 
Temperatures of more than 34°C is considered to be well above thermal comfort.

5.7.3 
Further considerations on indoor climate and energy 
• 
There are various models that take additional factors into account when assessing thermal 
indoor climate, including air velocity, relative humidity, the level of clothing and metabolic 
rate. However, the evaluation is kept simple for the performed simulations, as many factors 
should be considered before including these parameters. In case of a further developed  
• 
Openings has to be large enough and placed carefully to ventilate sufficiently, Night cooling 
could be considered  
• 
Ventilation aggregate should be designed with by-pass: 
o When temperatures within the canopy are high and natural ventilation is needed, 
the heat apartment exhaust air should not be running through the heat exchanger 
for the canopy supply air. 
o If the canopy are to be used for larger events requiring additional air change, a 
system that bypasses the apartments can be used, where direct exhaust from the 
canopy runs through the ventilation aggregate. In case of fire within the canopy this 
system can be used as active smoke vents and it should be considered if exhaust air 
from apartments should be set on hold, avoiding polluted air from the canopy to 
get into the apartments. 
• 
The above described solution require that the canopy air can be seen as fresh (outside) air. 
According to Peter Krantz (Bovieran caretaker) the fire safety within the Bovieran yard is 
seen as an outside room - maybe it is also possible when looking at ventilation 
requirements?  
• 
Although blower door tests, thermal photography and information about exhaust 
ventilation system has been used for the simulation model, it is still difficult to be sure 
about where the heat losses come from.  
• 
Solar shading could be applied at the canopy to avoid overheating 
• 
It is difficult to determine the extend heat losses through the construction 
• 
User behaviour can have a critical influence on the heat losses through open windows and 
doors. Curtains can influence the solar gains and windows facing balconies can be blocked 
by influenced by . The use of DHW can be varying. 
• 
Personalized heating within canopy – Benches, playground etc.  
• 
Low humidity within Canopy could be influenced by plants and vegetation as well as 
inclusion of an aquaponic system. 
• 
Ventilation: When supply air for the canopy is applied at the top zone (zone C7) the heat is 
not efficiently distributed to the occupied zone (C0). Ceiling fans can be used to mix the air. 
• 
Fresh inlet air could be forced down along the apartment facades in order to deliver the 
fresh air directly. Another strategy could be to blow the air down in the middle of the 
canopy – and follow the natural thermal updraft at the apartment facades. In this way, the 
air would pass the occupied zone in the canopy before reaching the apartments. Although 
this air flow is anticipated to be easiest to maintain, it could be considered as an 
unjustifiable solution, if the air is considered polluted after being in the occupied zone of 
the canopy.  
• 
Cost of mechanical ventilation system (installation and running fans should be considered) 
• 
Natural ventilation within apartments are setup to be optimistic with possibilities of 
ventilating 5 ACH at all times (with external air gradually between 23.5-27 °C.) 
==PAGE:139==
5.7 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 137 
 
 CONCLUSION  
PART 4:
Research on existing projects with similarities to the EKO-Canopy revealed the lightweight 
polymer-based material ETFE, as a profitable alternative choice of material to prefer over glass for 
the canopy roof. The smooth surface allow rain to wash off dirt from the material surface, which 
was one concern from the competition jury (Restvärmekonsortiet et al. 2015).  
The lightness and flexibility of the material allow forms that can help to reduce the surface area of 
the construction and a sloped construction that allow snow to drift off, with potential savings in 
material usage and energy consumption for snow melting. Geometry draft design proposals inspired 
by minimal surfaces was investigated and compared. With a minimal surface using the existing 
buildings as boundaries a surface reduction of about 20 % was found compared to the proposed 
design from the competition resulting in a surface area 8 % less than the existing building facades. 
The relatively large case study building with narrow courtyards show to have a good measures for an 
EKO Canopy. A modified form was found with a slope that could help solving issues about snow 
management.  
Wind flows and pressure on various geometries was assessed with Autodesk Flow Design. All 
investigated minimal surfaces showed relatively high negative pressure on the upper part of the 
constructions with wind directions from east and west. This will require efficient foundation of the 
structures, however good conditions for efficient natural ventilation openings was found and 
accelerated wind velocities at the top of the minimal constructions indicate good conditions for 
letting snow drift off the constructions. 
Daylight conditions within apartment rooms facing the canopy was investigated with Velux Daylight 
Visualizer. Simulations of Daylight Factors was assessed for critically located rooms with Nordic 
building regulations and the Swedish standard SS 914201 being the main focus for the 
investigations. Although the investigated rooms have more than sufficient sized windows, results 
shows that south oriented rooms have most critical Daylight Factors. The Daylight Factors was 
found to be far below the SS 914201 requirements, when balcony glazing was included. Results 
showed that daylight factors within these critically lit rooms will have increased daylight conditions if 
the balcony glazing are removed and a 3-or even 4-layer ETFE canopy construction installed 
instead. A mirror cladding material installed at the north oriented façade within the canopy has 
shown to increase daylight conditions considerably and at the same time give a positive architectural 
effect in the relatively narrow space between the two existing buildings. 
Energy performance and indoor climate investigations was performed with dynamic simulations 
using IESVE. Various scenarios ware investigated including scenarios. A basic 3-layer ETFE canopy 
construction showed to reduce heating needs within existing apartment to 65 % of the existing 
heating needs resulting in a total annual delivered energy of 100 kWh/m2 compared to the existing 
133 kWh/m2. This is when domestic hot water and electricity for installations are included.  
A principle of utilizing exhaust air from apartments to preheat supply air for the canopy has been 
investigated. Results show improved temperatures within the occupied canopy and avoids freezing 
temperatures. Higher energy improvements was found for a theoretical ventilation system where all 
supply air for apartments come preheated from the canopy. In this case the heating needs was found 
to be 45 % of the existing. Heating needs of 16 kWh/m2 and 18.4 kWh/m2 was simulated for 4-
layer and 3-layer ETFE constructions when combined with renovation of the external facades and 
windows. However this also include that all apartment supply air comes preheated from the canopy 
during times with heating. In case a sufficient solution for this is found, the EKO-Canopy concept 
can be seen as a profitable alternative to conventional renovation, when considering the possibilities 
for the new passively well-conditioned space between the existing buildings.  
==PAGE:140==
5.7 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 138 
 
 DISCUSSION  
PART 5:
ETFE qualities that can help solve the challenges with reduced daylight, cleaning of a canopy 
surface and allows forms that can reduce wind loads and help with snow management.  
The existing facades on Dragonvägen are worn. A new canopy covering allow installation of new 
façade cladding that doesn´t need to withstand the rough weather conditions, enabling a much 
higher freedom in choosing cladding type, including cheaper cladding that at the same time needs 
less maintenance. A material with high light reflectance is suggested in order to get enough sun light.  
The EKO-Canopy concept could be transferred to a large variety of apartment buildings  in Nordic 
counties. Inner yards in Copenhagen, where roof and façade renovations are applicable.  
Critical views on the concept could be included? 
It is possible to reach better insulating properties of a conventional renovated facade than what can 
be done with glass or ETFE. However, south oriented facades with balconies are difficult to 
renovate efficiently in a conventional way, as it is difficult to avoid the large cold bridges at 
balconies, whereas a total (Canopy) covering will be a good solution. 
Most aspects of the EKO-Canopy concept can be applied to other building types than the Million 
Homes Program apartment buildings, including application on existing as well as new buildings. The 
qualities of not interacting with the existing facades can be seen as an essential quality if the concept 
is applied onto protected buildings where existing façades need to be preserved. 
The wind load simulations showed considerable suction forces on the investigated design 
geometries. A sufficient foundation is considered to be important for a possible lightweight ETFE 
construction. A lightweight ETFE construction based on a wood and steel construction has shown 
to be possible. However, wood is likely to increase the weight of a canopy structure. But, if used 
wisely, would could be used as a way as counterweight in the construction to avoid extensive 
foundation. Local wood in Nordic countries is in general seen as an environmentally friendly 
building material that binds CO2 and by that can be a strategy to lower reduce the environmental 
impact. 
A socially sustainable renovation include that occupants can stay in the area during a renovation. 
Further more social sustainable renovation also take into account that low income households can 
afford stay after refurbishment. There are indications that the most successful EKO-Canopy project 
include a space that can be used by people in the neighbourhood and not just the people living the 
adjacent apartments. This will ensure social sustainability and avoid social exclusion. (Lind et al. 
2014) – See appendixxx “Future work – EKO-Canopy and social sustainability” 
The created space within the EKO-Canopy establishes a basis . The space can be adapted to the 
needs in the concerned area to improve the basis for existing activities as well as new activities. In 
order to meet the needs for social sustainability, the aspect of including the space within the existing 
environment is seen as essential for the success of the EKO-Canopy concept.  
Utilisation of the EKO-Canopy space 
• 
Urban gardening, aquaponics etc. - see also the winner of the competition - fish farming in 
abandoned industrial buildings combined with shops and restaurants. (SLU 2015) page 7-8 
See also Appendix

Sports – Find old list… 
• 
Temporary accommodation during renovation

This section tries to give a rough estimate of construction costs of an EKO-Canopy and compare it 
with a conventional energy renovation project of a similar 7 stories apartment building. By  
“conventional” energy renovation, it is meant improving the thermal envelope for lower U-values 
and the technical installations for more energy sufficient services, to limit the energy consumption 
for the entire building. 
It is difficult to know the expenses for a construction before the design is chosen. However, various 
prices are found during research and are shown in Table 5-1. The prices are all calculated on basis of 
a simple rectangular canopy construction of 3270m2 corresponding to the Dragonvägen courtyard.

given directly from NOWOFOL estimated on basis of the actual measures.  
5.1.2 
Conventional renovation 
Renovation of AKB Sjælør Boulevard in Denmark is used for comparison to a conventional energy 
renovation. The project was finished by White Architects in September 2015. The detailed project 
budget has been used to estimate prices for renovation of the façade.  
The façade areas of two Dragonvägen building blocks are calculated to be 3135 m2 of which 945 m2 
are windows or balcony doors. The south façade include approximately double the area of windows 
as the north facade.  
Possible expenses due to treatment of Asbestos in a conventional renovation has not been included 
in these calculations, whereas the expenses for a conventional renovation is expected to be 
significantly higher (Asbestforening 2010) It is possible that a conventional  
==PAGE:142==

5.1.3 
Cost comparison 
To give an overview of the prices of the different types of renovation and material for the canopy a 
simple estimation is made. The prices have been estimated from the several sources previously 
mentioned having the rectangular canopy as base for the size. The simple graph in Table ?? shows 
the cost comparisons in million € for the renovation expenses. 
 
From the simple economical overview, it the total envelope renovation (conventional) is a bit more 
expensive than a façade renovation with an ETFE canopy, whereas the façade renovation with a 
Glass canopy is a bit more expensive than the conventional renovation. For the simple economical 
perspective, the ETFE canopy is beneficial as an addition to the façade renovation. 
5.2 
Environmental impact considerations 
• 
Demolishing and construction of a new building are considered for many “The Million 
Homes Program – Develop or demolish” (Formas et al. 2012) 
• 
Glass 40 kg/m2 construction, glass and steel (smaller span, ) 
•

result in 1050 kg of ETFE whereas x % is fluorine, resulting in x kg of fluorine. This is x % 
of the annual mining.

is used in cookware. About 180,000 metric tons of fluoropolymers were produced in 2006 
and 2007 Wiki

•

solution. For the purpose of energy savings, there are several possible strategies.  
•

pump, PV’s and solar thermal panels. PV’s can be added as energy production source.  
• 
Heating strategies: 
• 
Passive heat can either be solar gains, misc or  heat recovery.

5.3.1 
Integrated PV´s 
• 
PV integration in glass roof coverings has been done in many projects before, including 
several Solar Decathlon Projects – See EMBRACE reference project. The efficiency has 
shown to be good, but the heaviness of the construction and the required thick safety glass 
layer needed for the roof coverings reduces the efficiency. Source? 
• 
Integration of PV´s within ETFE cushions has already been done. The weather resistance 
and high light transmission of the ETFE material makes it a very interesting covering for 
PV´s. See Munich Car park section xxx.  
• 
Flexible transparent panels from Alsvid Solar – potential cooperation between Alsvid Solar 
and Vector Foiltec.  
• 
A contact between Alsvid Solar and Vector Foiltec has been established as a result of this 
thesis work 
 
Figure 5-1 Semi-transparent, organic, light, thin and flexible PV´s from Alsvid Solar (alsvid.com)  
5.3.2 
Seasonal thermal energy storage (STES)  
The initial concept idea of the EKO-Canopy by White Architects included a suggestion of using 
excess heat combined with heat storage. 
”Application of seasonal thermal energy storage with heat pumps for heating and cooling buildings has received much 
consideration in recent decades, as it can help to cover gaps between energy availability and demand, e.g. from summer 
to winter. This has the potential to reduce the large proportion of energy consumed by buildings, especially in colder 
climate countries. The problem with seasonal storage, however, is heat loss. This can be reduced by low-temperature 
storage but a heat pump is then recommended to adjust temperatures as needed by buildings in use. The aim of this 
paper was to compare different seasonal thermal energy storage methods using a heat pump in terms of coefficient of 
performance (COP) of heat pump and solar fraction, and further, to investigate the relationship between those factors 
and the size of the system, i.e. collector area and storage volume based on past building projects including residences, 
offices and schools.” Abstract from “Seasonal thermal energy storage with heat pumps and low temperatures in 
building projects—A comparative review”  link 
==PAGE:144==
5.3 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 142 
 
5.3.3 
EKO-Canopy and urban gardening 
The proposal by White Architects included ideas about urban gardening and aquapoic systems 
within the EKO-Canopy.  
 
Figure 5-2 Diagram from (Grain 2011) The numbers are calculated taking the whole lifecycle of the food 
industry into account and states that between the food industry is responsible for 44-57% of the global 
greenhouse gas emmisions.  
• 
The article “Food and climate change: the forgotten link” from (Grain 2011) states that the 
food industry constitutes an even larger GWP than the building industry. Coupling the two 
major GWP contributors could/can make basis for long term solutions of the major 
challenges we are facing (regarding the global warming).  
• 
The environmental impacts included are the processing, transport, packing and retail of 
food.  
• 
As a result of the increased knowledge about the emissions related to food production, 
urban gardening is becoming increasingly more popular. In Copenhagen urban gardening is 
most commonly seen on rooftops or in public parks, where areas are free for locals to use 
(Byhaven NV and Østergro). Byhaven 2200, Østergro, Byhaven NV.   
• 
Just a little. Maize field in downtown manhattan,  
• 
Aquaponics – a way to save energy for food production, transport. 
• 
Half of the life in the ocean has been depleted due to overfishing during the past 40 years 
(ing.dk). As the ocean contains about 80 % of all life on earth and many people are 
depending on it (overfishing.org), overfishing is a major problem. 
==PAGE:145==
5.3 - Energy performance and indoor climate 
Page 143 
 
 
Figure 5-3 Diagram showing the principal of an aquaponic system. The system include a fish tank and a bed 
for plants where water is circulated with a piping system. Waste water from the fish tank works as fertilizer for 
the plants, cleaning the water, which can be circulated back into the fish tank (Source: 
howtomakeaquaponics.blogspot.dk) 
The climate within the EKO-Canopy form good conditions for Urban gardening and there are 
many examples showing favourable results. Moisture production could be a concern. A covered 
space within the canopy for the system could be needed, like it has been done in the Khan Shatyr 
Entertainment Centre.  
Integration of urban gardening in a public space like the EKO-Canopy will require several 
considerations. Although there are many good examples of well working urban gardening projects, it 
is considered to be important that the integration is very well thought through.  
It is not the goal that the EKO-Canopy can establish self sufficient food production for the people 
living in the concerned building blocks, but it can help getting things in the right direction and 
increase the focus on sustainable food production and the environment in general.

5.3 - Future work 
Page 144 
 
6 
Future work  
• 
Further investigations on structure: Large construction with large span -> A lot to save if 
this is becoming a popular concept-product like the Bovieran product – Master thesis using 
Autodesk Revit Dynamo to optimize the construction 
• 
Further investigations on indoor climate: 
o Air flows within canopy and air flows exchange between canopy and apartments 
o Moisture conditions within the canopy - Can plants and vegetation help to stabilize 
the moisture level – e.g. with a aquaponic system 
o Can plants help to absorb air pollution and clean the air within the canopy? 
o Measures on solar shading and natural ventilation 
• 
Energy measures: 
o Use of excess heat for improved temperatures within the canopy and lower 
ventilation losses.  
o Integration of PV’s within ETFE cushions to be used in combination with solar 
shading control – optimisation of the canopy geometry for maximized production  
• 
Investigations on ground temperature influence and possible inclusion of seasonal thermal 
energy storage, STES (e.g. COMSOL or HEAT2) 
• 
More detailed analysis of LCA and LCC 
• 
Investigations on political and social aspects (Architects and other?) 
• 
How to implement to EKO-Canopy in a residential environment in a way that satisfy as 
many people as possible. Investigations of how to integrate people and utilize the space in 
the best possible way. Urban gardening, aquaponics and organized food is a suggestion with 
good potentials.

Competition 
“Swedish University of Agricultural Sciences (SLU) in Alnarp has, with support from Vinnova and with 
residual heat Consortium SSE-C (Swedish Surplus Energy Collaboration), the City of Malmö, Lund 
Municipality, County Administrative Board of Skåne, E.ON Sweden, Kraftringen AB and the Swedish

2. 
Feedback on EKO-Canopy 
Feedback on White Architects proposal within the competition “Innovative utilisation of excess heat in 
future cities” 
 
 
Competition winner “Fish Farm” 
First prize 150 000 SEK for professionals Fish Farm Proposer: Martin Ekelin, Fredrik Forsman, Niklas 
Svensson and Martin Wetterstedt - Greenelizer  
This proposal presents an appealing mixed city's vision of neighbourhood scale, industrial, housing and 
services combined. The proposer takes us to an area of town that needs to be vitalized. A residual heat 
heated fish farm is established in an abandoned industrial building and become the core of an urban 
renewal process by vegetable farming, accommodation, restaurant and market gradually developed as part 
of an overall concept. 
Residual heat usage for fish farming and vegetable cultivation is well known, but the build-up of the 
business in stages, and that it should be in close-urban environment in existing buildings makes the 
proposal more innovative. The successive cultivation of plants make the proposal reliable and realistic to 
implement. Exploiting fish farming water tanks that heat storage is a new idea, but the practical, economic 
and technical usefulness is doubtful since fish can not tolerate particularly large fluctuations in temperature 
nor special high maximum temperatures. The conditions for combining aquaculture with housing needs to 
be clarified with regard to risks for odor, hygienic aspects, etc. 
The design is clearly recognized by educational pictures while gradually describes the development of the 
business over time. The proposal would increase the attractiveness of the city near the small industrial areas 
through "gentrification" can be interesting for example for housing, shops and restaurants. The proposal 
also has a strong social dimension by developing businesses that create jobs but also trade and restaurants 
that provide meeting places.  
Among Urban idea of the combination of jobs, homes and wellbeing in the local environment provides a 
direct connection to the smart, sustainable city. Especially as residual heat utilization used in a concept for 
creating attractiveness in run-down neighbourhoods.” (SLU, 2015) page 7-8

3. 
Dragonvägen - Thermal photography & reference pictures 
Assessment of thermal photography has been performed by “Energikompetens AB” according to Swedish 
building standard SS/EN 13187. The following include selected thermal photography. Comments are 
added.  
Thermography is a qualitative method for detecting abnormalities in the building envelope, such as 
infiltration (leakages), insulation deficiencies and thermal bridges. The thermal images are presented as 
thermography where colder surfaces are darker and warmer surfaces are lighter colour. Light colours on 
outside photos show where the thermal transmission is highest, whereas high thermal transmission is 
shown with dark colours at photos taken from the inside. Temperatures are shown with colour scale at the 
right of each image. (based on the report from “Energikompetens AB”) 
Following conditions was measured at the time of the assessment (17/4-2013 9-10am) 
• 
Weather: Partly cloudy to clear, facade partly sun-exposed,  
• 
Wind: between 1-2 m/s. 
• 
Outside temperature: Approximately 9 ° C 
• 
Internal temperature: 24 ° C 
 
West gable wall - Light colours around windows 
show high thermal leakage from window frames. 
Balconies are located at south (right side of the 
photography), where thermal bridges are found. 
Outdoor thermal photography 
 
 
West gable wall. Reference picture 
 
 
North oriented façade. Highest locations seem to 
have the lowest temperatures. (Possibly due to higher 
wind exposure and less heat radiation from surroundings.) 
Outdoor thermal photography 
 
 
North oriented façade. Reference picture 
(Note: the reflectance in the windows mainly show the 
temperature of what is being reflected)

11
 
Light corners indicate thermal bridges at the 
construction joints. 
Outdoor thermal photography 
 
 
Resessed building corner at ground floor. 
Reference picture 
 
 
Thermal leakage at ceiling/wall joint. 
Internal thermal photography  
 
 
Apartment ceiling corner. Reference picture 
 
 
Thermal leakage at floor/wall joint.

The very high temperature difference of about 
12°C indicate air leakage at the door frame. This 
kind of thermal leakage is seen on all assessed 
balcony doors.

4. 
Dragonvägen – Blower door test 
Blower door tests have been performed in accordance with SS/EN 13829 by “Energikompetens AB”. 
Following conditions were measured at the time of the assessment: 
 
• 
Indoor temperature: 23 ° C 
• 
Outdoor temperature: 9 ° C 
• 
Wind speed: About 1-2 m/s 
Blower door test results for 50 Pa pressure: 
 
The test results of 0.258 l/s and 0.392 l/s per m2 envelope area, has been calculated to be corresponding 
with 0.86 l/s 1.20 l/s respectively per floor area, using a room height of 2.45m. 
a) 
Dragonvägen multiple challenges 
1. The houses needed to be renovated and energy efficient 
2. The houses had totally dead ground floors, miserable yards and all public spaces around the houses 
and boring park spaces in between 
3. Socially stigmatized area 
4. Repetitive apartments, almost all were 3 rooms on 74 squares

2. 
SIRen meeting program 
 
Participation in a SIRen workshop 4th December 2015 has been a part of the thesis research. The 
attendance included a presentation of the recent thesis investigations of the EKO-Canopy project and 
attained information about the newest research within the Swedish built environment. SIRen (Sustainable 
Integrated Ren-ovation) is a Swedish inter-disciplinary research environment for holistic sustainable 
renovation. The organisation is driven by researchers from universities and business collaborators and 
investors (renoveringscentrum.lth.se).

==PAGE:172==
 
23
 
a) 
ETFE solar control (Vector Foiltec) 
 
Prints on the ETFE cushions are considered for the EKO-Canopy to reduce overheating during the hot 
season. Prints seems to reduce the light transmittance critically, whereas a combination of transparent 
cushions combined with prints are considered a good settlement.  
In case a “Vario” solution is considered, the “Squares” print seems to be the most efficient system to 
control the solar gains with a 45 % reduction from 0.42 to 0.23 in g-value. The “Dot Random” does only 
have a 32 % reduction in solar gain control from 0.44 to 0.30. The control of U-value can be an additional 
factor to consider if the “Vario” system is chosen to be taken into use.

A minimal surface will always generate the smallest surface area with the given boundary conditions, but the 
benefits when it comes to surface area savings varies according to the given structure. A building with half 
the length of the Dragonvägen building blocks will have larger benefits of using a minimal surface. 
 
Adding new building mass to the existing million program houses can have several advantages. New 
luxurious apartments can attract people with higher income paying high rents, resulting in short payback 
time for added building mass. This can help flatten out the social disintegration of the million program 
areas. New shops, cafes and other institutions can cultivate a new lively and social atmosphere within and 
around the building.

==PAGE:176==
 
27
1. 
Canopy wind load investigations 
a) 
Q/A With GEA Fluid Mechanics Specialist 
 
Theory Q/A with: Henrik Brun Hansen Fluid Mechanics Specialist at GEA Process Engineering A/S 
Umiddelbart lader det til at programmet selv genererer et mesh før den begynder simuleringen, men det er ikke muligt at se det 
genererede mesh. Jeg tænker at dette må være en god grund til at være kritisk overfor programmets troværdighed...?  
Lad mig allerførst sige at Ansys Fluent - og i øvrigt de andre Ansys produkter Design Modeller og Mesher - som 
vi brugte i!CFD!kurset, ikke er verdens allerlækreste stykker software. De er heller ikke blevet ordentligt opdateret 
siden 90erne! Der er ingen tvivl om at Autodesk har fat i den lange ende mht. brugervenlighed. Her har Ansys 
mfl. meget at lære. 
 
1)      Hvor fint skal et mesh være for at det kan betragtes som "godt nok"? Det har ikke været muligt for mig at finde information 
om hvilke beregninger programmet benytter - er der nogle vigtige beregninger som simpelthen udelades? - Eller hvilke input-parametre, 
som ikke kan justeres i programmet som er den største mangel?  
Meshing er lige dele videnskab og religion!☺!. J. Fan som har!CFD!kurset (ham kineseren) mener at meshing er 
vigtigere end livet selv, men jeg har haft undervisere som ikke bruger 10 minutter på det. Jeg tror dog at alle vil 
være enige om, at dårlige mesh giver dårlige resultater. Det handler bestemt ikke kun om ”finhed” (antallet af 
elementer per m^3), men også om kvalitet – det vil sige – er elementerne nogenlunde geometrisk flotte eller er de 
vildt skæve (skewness)? Det handler også om fordelingen af elementer – har vi jævnt fordelte elementer eller har 
vi lagt alle elementer der hvor der er gradienter i flowet? Normalt er det en god ide at have ekstra mange 
elementer tæt på vægge fordi turbulensmodellernes validitet afhænger af opløsningen tæt på overflader. Autodesk 
vil ikke lade dig styre det – så vi kan ikke vide om disse ting bliver OK!!" 
 
2)       Ligesom med Ansys, har jeg erfraret at dette program også simulere i et stykke tid, før en steady state tilstand opnås, hvilket 
jeg ser som et tegn på at den trods alt benytter nogle af de samme algoritmiske analyser som andre CFD programmer - hvad tænker 
du om dette?  
Autodesk bruger helt sikkert bare Steady State Reynolds Averaged Navier-Stokes formuleringer (Steady RANS). 
Hvilken turbulensmodel der bruges ved jeg ikke – et bud er k-epsilon eller k-omega. Udelader programmet 
derved vigtige beregninger? Nej! Men det er ret sikkert at den ikke regner til bunds, da det ville kræve: a.      Styring 
af beregningsforholdene (input parametre til solveren såsom Under Relaxation Factors, inputs til 
turbulensmodellerne, styring af mesh bla bla bla) b.      Mange flere beregningsressourcer end dødelige mennesker 
har… En kæmpe computer. 
 
3)      Ved du om der er nogle situationer, i forhold til vindsimulering og tryk på bygningsflader, hvor der er grund til at være ekstra 
skeptisk overfor flow design? 
JA! Hvis du har komplicerede geometrier. Skarpe hjørner, hurtigt accelerende flow, høje tryk, store gradienter. 
Eller noget der krummer ”meget”. (hvor meget er ”meget”?). 
Generelt er min vurdering af du kan bruge Autodesks programmer til at få indtryk af størrelsesforhold til 
sammenligning af designs, men du kan ikke bruge det til at påstå at du har lavet en grundig flowmæssig analyse. 
Til det skal du bruge Ansys Fluent. Eller sådan nogen som mig. 
Ansys lancerer snart et helt nyt program som hedder ”Aim”. Her vil de lave noget lignende Autodesk Flow, men 
med Fluents solver og mulighed for styring af alting. Jeg mener at de er klar med noget beta-testing af det – og 
via Ansys student har universitetsstuderende vist adgang til alle Ansys programmer nu. (Det er helt nyt) – jeg ved 
ikke om du har tid og lyst – men hvis du har – kunne du undersøge om der var noget at tage fat på her? 
Ps. Det ville være elegant hvis du i din afhandling lavede et lille appendix med simulering af en simpel facade 
med hhv. Autodesk Flow og Ansys Fluent. Så kan du jo ud fra det skrive lidt om, om dine simuleringer er 
nogenlunde troværdige! 
 
Additional information is found on 
•

•

• 
Flow around Buildings Link  
•

D. 
Daylight  
1. 
Annual solar radiation 
 
Full year simulation of solar radiation on Dragonvägen building blocks, simulated with IESVE. 
 
The simulation show highest solar energy gains at the roof with values around 930 kWh/m2. Windows at 
lowest positioned floors receives the smallest annual solar energy gains of about 330 kWh/m3.  
Gables seem to receive higher annual energy gains than what is seen at the south oriented windows. 
However, no shading surfaces are included at the gables.  
2. 
Certifications 
a) 
Swedish Standard SS 914201 
The table below show daylight required DF for existing buildings according to Swedish Maljöbyggnad 
certification (Miljöbyggnad, 2014). Requirements for new buildings are the same.  
 
b) 
DGNB certification 
This section is based on “DGNB System Denmark – Apartments buildings and row houses“ (translated from 
Danish) (GBCD, 2014) page 188-194 Link  
DGNB-DK is a relatively new building certification system in Denmark, adapted from the German 
certification system through Green Building Council Denmark (GBCD). The system is widespread in 
Europe although Denmark is currently the only Nordic country using the DGNB system.  
Visual comfort is assessed by the following indicators: 
1. Daylight in the building 
2. Daylight on permanent working places / at the home 
3. View to the outside 
==PAGE:182==
 
33
4. No glare of daylight (NB: Not included for residential buildings) 
5. No glare from electric lighting (NB: Not included for residential buildings) 
6. Light distribution from electric lighting (NB: Not included for residential buildings) 
7. Colour Rendering 
8. Sunlight 
“View to the outside plays an essential role for occupants satisfaction level with the apartment”  
 
 
==PAGE:183==
 
34
 
 
c) 
Active house 
The active house is an organisation supported by DTU, Aalborg University and a list of companies, 
including Velux, Rockwool, Saint Gobain and more. Active House are designed for residential buildings. 
Link to more infod 
DF is measured at a horizontal work plane approx. 0.8 m. Each factor is weighted to give an average 
daylight factor for each room. 
Furthermore, Active House also include score points for having direct sunlight inside the building

4. 
Surface material properties 
Sbi 230´s recommended values for reflectance (Used when reflectance are not known)  
• 
Ceilings 0.7  
• 
Walls 0.4  
• 
Floors 0.1 
In VDV there is an option of adjusting “Specularity” (=Shininess) and “Roughness” (=Bumpiness) for non 
transparent materials. These adjustments can give more detailed analysis when specific materials are known. 
As these daylight studies are performed to indicate the influence on daylight of various design/material 
solutions the specularity and roughness are kept constant. As all building materials have some roughness, a 
value of 0.030 is chosen which is the same as for a plastic surface. The specularity of is set to zero.  
The IOR ( Index of Refraction) values are set to 1.5 which are close to all three material types. See table 
below: 
IOR - Index of Refraction 
values 
Air 
1.00* 
Acrylic glass 
1.49* 
Glass 
1.50* 
Plastic 
1.46* 
ETFE 
1.40** 
PTFE 
1.35** 
Source *Link **(Gooch, 2011) page 617-618

5. 
Grid points 
Grid points are measured in millimetres within the SketchUp VDV model. X, Y and Z coordinates from 
this model are entered within Excel. Vector coordinates are required for the grid point file, with 0-values 
for X and Y and 1 for the Z axis. The mm are converted to meters and combined to a single column. This 
has been done to be able to copy paste the numbers into WordPad in windows, which seemed to be the 
only way to make the grid point file work. 
Plotted grid point values are shown in the figure below.

VDV test of rendering quality and resolution 
The DEVSQ function in excel is used to assess the deviations of the test simulation results. The Excel 
DEVSQ function calculates the sum of the squared deviations from the sample mean for a supplied set of 
values (Excel). The best results showed to be simulation setup with

7. 
VDV test with glazing angles 
VDV does not include information about how it calculates transmittance through angled surfaces. It is 
possible to adjust the IOR value, which is set to the default value of 1.500 within all simulations in the 
thesis report. Some test simulations has been performed with various placement and angle of a glazing.  
 
The boxes measures 1x1x2 m. The glazing is positioned as shown; at the top, in the middle and at an angle 
of 45°. All glazing has a transmittance of 0.50. The three boxes at the top is simulated with 1.00 reflectance 
and the three boxes blow are simulated with 0.00 reflectance. The DF is measured at a horizontal plane at a 
height of 0.1 m height. Point DF values are given in each corner and in the middle of each render image. 
Points are inserted manually, whereas accuracy is uncertain for these values. However, ISO contour lines 
are included for further assessment.  
 
 
With a IOR value of 1.000 the results seems to be the same for the simulations, no matter where the 
glazing is placed and oriented.  
==PAGE:190==
 
41
 
With an IOR value of 1.500 results seems to be the same for the simulations with the horizontal orientation 
at different heights.  
Results of the angled glazing (image to the right), indicate that the angling combined with a higher IOR 
value results in a reduction of LT.  
The test simulations indicate that VDV include further reduction in LT for light transmitted through angled 
glazing, when IOR values are set higher than 1.000. A value of 1.500 is used for all simulations within the 
report.

48
e) 
Transparent balcony banister 
Example of transparent balcony banister guards. 
The idea is to let in more daylight and give better 
visual outlook with more contact to the outside. 
This idea can be considered if facades are to be 
renovated and more daylight is needed.  
Both glass and stretched metal could be 
considered. Existing metal balcony railing sheets 
could be converted into stretched metal sheets. 
Stretching of metal sheets will in general result in 
about 30% elongation. The additional metal could 
be melted and reused.
